Mouthpiece Work / Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
The initial testing is completed with results exactly as expected.  I'll be posting the study in the next couple of days.  Here's a preview:

This study shows that what A. Benade terms as the "Effective Mouthpiece Volume", of a saxophone mouthpiece on a cylindrical tube*, is actually the exact average of the actual phase-dependent, "reed open" and "reed closed" volumes of the mouthpiece, for each cycle of the waveform.  Awareness of the maximum and minimum volume values establishes the range within which the mouthpiece functions normally.  One would think that Benade's averaging would be sufficiently accurate to enable the conscientious tech or player to effectively match a mouthpiece to a saxophone, however, once we see that the actual volume of the mouthpiece is at any one time reed phase dependent, and we compare the reed phase characteristics of cylindrical air columns with those of conical air columns, it becomes clear that Benade's Effective Mouthpiece Volume, calculated on a cylindrical tube, does not transfer effectively to the conical saxophone without some compensation and
 consideration for saxophone size, design, and playing style.

Part I describes the initial test procedure and it's results.  Part II deals with the differences between cylindrical and conical air columns and reed cycles and how to best match a saxophone's missing cone volume and Frs requirements with a mouthpiece's Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range and Frs, for the various saxophone sizes, designs, and types of playing.  A simplified and supremely accurate Frs method is explained.

FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
You are to be complimented for the amount of time and effort that went
into your study Lance.  Now that I have had some time to study what you
have done, I would like to offer a couple of friendly suggestions.

1. Think about the equivalent mouthpiece volume of the saxophone when
played at softer levels when the reed does not close completely and how
that ties in with your theory that the closed and open volumes are
"averaged" to give the equivalent volume and therefore the pitch.

2. Have a closer look at the relationship between the equivalent volume
inside the mouthpiece and the ratio between the opening and closing time
of the reed.

3. Look at the possibility that Benade made a mistake in his class notes
and the Frs of the mouthpiece + neck should have been given as the 1st
mode of the shortest tube used---not the second.  This would agree with
the actual played pitch of the alto neck at its written F2, and the
played pitch of the tenor sax mouthpiece and neck at its written F#2.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Conn Microtuner: for John and Lance
Hi Steve,

Here is the thing: we know that the sax tunes basically not only by length, but by how well the mpc mimics the missing end of the cone, which is cut off at the end of the neck to have a place to put the mpc/reed so you can actually blow the thing.

Basically, when you push the mpc onto the cork, it reduces the volume inside the mpc (the neck sticks further in) and this raises the pitch, and vice-versa. The trouble is that this doesn't raise the pitch equally--higher notes get more sharp than lower notes. This means that really, for optimum
 intonation between registers, there is only one ideal position of the mpc on the cork, but depending on other factors, such as temperature and embouchure and certain reed characteristics (and of course the design of the horn itself), this may or may not be when the horn is playing in the correct
 pitch.

Further, even if everything is perfect, bands tune to different versions of A. So while it is absolutely convenient to be able to move the mpc on the neck to tune the horn based on all these conditions, it can throw the octaves out, or make the palms crazy sharp (or sometimes flat).

But what if you could change the length of the horn without changing the inside volume of the mpc? Benade says (referring to oboes) that reducing the volume inside the reed raises pitch, but not equally in the registers, while shortening the reed raises pitch pretty much equally across the entire
 range of the horn. 

Unfortunately, this is not easy to do with a sax. With an oboe you can just use a shorter staple, which is analogous to using a shorter neck on a sax, but how many people carry necks of different sizes? But aha! The Conn microtuner actually acts as a variable-length neck. The mpc is pushed
 completely on the cork, and the microtuner varies the length of the neck pipe.

So here is the perfect way to test the idifference between moving the mpc on the cork and changing the length of the neck while not varying the internal volume of the mpc, as it relates to intonation.

This is difficult to get precise without the use of a stable (probably artificial) embouchure. But what would be interesting is to do this:

1. Push the mpc completely on the cork, and then blow the horn with the microtuner at its shortest and longest position. Find out how well the registers are in relation and the pitch in three octaves. Blow, say, C1-C2-C3 through F1-F2-F3. If Benade is correct (or if I am correct in assuming that
 this can be applied to the sax), those three octave notes should be somewhat true octaves, no matter whether the microtuner is completely extended and the horn plays its flattest, or completely retracted and the horn plays its sharpest.

2. Now put the microtuner at its shortest and instead reproduce the pitch played with the microtuner at its longest by pulling the mpc off the cork like a normal sax. It is not necessary to get it exactly the same length, just pull it off until the middle octave is playing the same pitch as it was
 with the mpc fully on the cork with the microtuner out all the way.

3. Play those same octave jumps as before now and check and see how the octaves play. Theory would say that the octaves should be narrowed, meaning that the upper notes should play flatter than they did just using the microtuner.

The microtuner thus gives us a very good way of checking the difference between tuning by changing mpc volume (and length to a smaller extent) against tuning solely by changing the length of the air column.

Toby

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Thanks John,

I've been planning to upate and expand the study.   Of course, low dynamic playing, where the reed does not close, the volumes will be different.  Also, during the time the reed is open, it is constantly moving.  The "reed open" volume that I obtained from the "reed closed" and the EMV average, is actually the AVERAGE volume of the reed open phase.  So the actual "reed open" volume fluctuates between almost closed to 5.68 ml over, the given "reed open" volume - so a much wider range.  Crazy.

--- On Fri, 4/9/10, John <jtalcott47@...> wrote:

You are to be complimented for the amount of time and effort that went into your study Lance.  Now that I have had some time to study what you have done, I would like to offer a couple of friendly suggestions.

1. Think about the equivalent mouthpiece volume of the saxophone when played at softer levels when the reed does not close completely and how that ties in with your theory that the closed and open volumes are "averaged" to give the equivalent volume and therefore the pitch.

2. Have a closer look at the relationship between the equivalent volume inside the mouthpiece and the ratio between the opening and closing time of the reed.

3. Look at the possibility that Benade made a mistake in his class notes and the Frs of the mouthpiece + neck should have been given as the 1st mode of the shortest tube used---not the second.  This would agree with the actual played pitch of the alto neck at its written F2, and the played pitch of the tenor sax mouthpiece and neck at its written F#2.

FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
I don't think you understand the point I am trying to get across.  If
the effective mouthpiece volume when the reed is being closed completely
is an average of the mouthpiece open volume and the mouthpiece closed
volume, then it is that volume which determines the length of the cone
the wave "sees" and determines its pitch.

Under your theory, when the reed does not have a closed cycle at softer
levels, no averaging takes place, and the mouthpiece volume is
determined by just the reed open phase.  That would mean that at softer
levels of playing the volume is larger creating a pitch that is lower,
and then when the dynamic level rises to where the reed closes each
cycle, the mouthpiece equivalent volume suddenly drops to the
"average"causing the pitch to suddenly go higher.  In actual playing
this does not happen during a crescendo, and the reverse does not happen
during a diminuendo.  There is never a sudden change in pitch going in
and out of the closed reed cycle,  although there is a perceptible
change in timbre.

How do you explain this under your "theory"?

It is important to remember that the mouthpiece "effective volume" is
what the sound wave "sees" as the length of the missing cone to its
apex, and that it is this virtual length that determines the pitch of
each note.  I don't see how that volume and length can keep changing
throughout its range as you assert and allow a saxophone to be played in
tune.

Again, the equivalent volume of the mouthpiece is not a function of the
ratio of the amount of time the reed is open or closed.  Those are two
completely different physical aspects of the workings of the mouthpiece
and reed.  Remember that it is the missing cone of the saxophone, not
its missing array of cones of different sizes for different notes and
dynamic levels. It is difficult enough already to try to match one
mouthpiece equivalent volume to that of the missing cone, and now you
are trying to say there are dozens of different mouthpiece equivalent
volumes.

F#2.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"If 
the effective mouthpiece volume when the reed is being closed completely
 is an average of the mouthpiece open volume and the mouthpiece 
closed volume, then it is that volume which determines the length of the
 cone the wave "sees" and determines its pitch."

Benade's Effective Mouthpiece Volume does not exist within the waveform cycle as it is only the product of the 2 phases of a completed cycle.  During the cycle, the actual volume of the "reed closed" phase is what it is.  That static minimal volume determines the length of the tube for the time it takes the wave to go two entire lengths (cylindrical), or (conical) for the length of time it takes the mouthpiece + constriction resonance, to go from the truncation, to the theoretical apex, and back, and all tube resonances react accordingly.  Because of the additive nature of the cycle and the way we perceive pitch, the parts of the cycle are added together and divided by the duration.  The end perception of a completed cycle does not change the actual reality of what happens within the cycle.  One thing is not the other.

"Under your
 theory, when the reed does not have a closed cycle at softer levels, no
 averaging takes place, and the mouthpiece volume is determined by just 
the reed open phase.  That would mean that at softer levels of playing 
the volume is larger creating a pitch that is lower, and then when the 
dynamic level rises to where the reed closes each cycle, the mouthpiece 
equivalent volume suddenly drops to the "average"causing the pitch to 
suddenly go higher."

As I just posted earlier, I have to update the study.  In any "reed open" phase, the reed is moving all over the place, and what we calculate as "reed open" volume in the EMV test is actually the average of all the "reed open" volumes within that phase of the cycle.  Only the "reed closed" volume is static  The EMV volume of a completed cycle then, is the average of the "reed open" volumes, averaged with itself and the static "reed closed" volume.  As the "reed open" volume is always being averaged our perception of the completed cycle, the pitch does not change.

"It is important to 
remember that the mouthpiece "effective volume" is what the sound wave 
"sees" as the length of the missing cone to its apex, and that it is 
this virtual length that determines the pitch of each note.  I 
don't see how that volume and length can keep changing throughout its 
range as you assert and allow a saxophone to be played in tune."

Sorry.  Again, the sound wave and tube resonances do not perceive themselves as we perceive them.  They exist in the moment, determined by the actual volume of the mouthpiece in that segment of time (within the range of the speed of sound).  EMV is a result of a completed cycle, not the cycles individual components.

"Again,
 the equivalent volume of the mouthpiece is not a function of the ratio 
of the amount of time the reed is open or closed."

Be aware that Benade did not include time domain considerations in his method, however, they exist non-the-less, and are certainly worth considering, if an accurate picture of what is going on is important.

"It is difficult enough already to try to match one 
mouthpiece equivalent volume to that of the missing cone, and now you 
are trying to say there are dozens of different mouthpiece equivalent 
volumes."

Benade's method does not consider time nor what happens within each cycle.  I choose not to limit myself to that degree.  If you understand everything that is going on, you realize that while the missing cone is a set volume, the mouthpiece can only offer a shifting compromise, but, with that understanding the application of that shifting compromise can be realized optimally for any given situation.
FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
That static minimal volume determines the length of the tube for the
time it takes the wave to go two entire lengths (cylindrical), or
(conical) for the length of time it takes the mouthpiece + constriction
resonance, to go from the truncation, to the theoretical apex, and back,
and all tube resonances react accordingly.

Exactly what do these statements mean Lance?   "The mouthpiece +
constriction resonance". "That all tube resonances react accordingly".  
As often is the case, after you have answered my questions I am more
confused than I was before I asked them.

Only the "reed closed" volume is static  The EMV volume of a completed
cycle then, is the average of the "reed open" volumes, averaged with
itself and the static "reed closed" volume.  As the "reed open" volume
is always being averaged our perception of the completed cycle, the
pitch does not change.

This fails to address the issue that I raised that as the mouthpiece
equivalent volume goes from the value of the average of the open and
closed volumes (even if the open volume is itself an average) to the
constant open volume there will be an abrupt change in the mouthpiece
equivalent volume which in turn will create an abrupt change in the
pitch.  The following chain of relationships should be taken into
account and not be ignored in this discussion.

Mouthpiece equivalent volume ---volume of missing cone---length of
missing cone---wavelength of note---pitch of note

Benade's method does not consider time nor what happens within each
cycle.  I choose not to limit myself to that degree.  If you understand
everything that is going on, you realize that while the missing cone is
a set volume, the mouthpiece can only offer a shifting compromise, but,
with that understanding the application of that shifting compromise can
be realized optimally for any given situation.

Even if you are correct in your first (as yet unproven) assumption that
the MEV as you call it is the result of averaging what takes place in
the time domain of parts of the wave's cycle your second assumption that
the MEV is dependent upon the ratio of the opening and closing time of
the reed is completely false.

It is true that the ratio between the closing and opening time of the
reed is equal to the ratio of the truncation (length of missing cone) to
the tube length (to the first open tonehole).  What you are missing
completely is the fact that it is the length of the tube past the end of
the neck that is changing to alter the ratio of truncation---not the
length/volume of the missing cone or its substitution.  That
length/volume (of the mouthpiece substitution) is quite constant over a
considerable fraction of an instrument's playing range.  Benade FMA p.
467.

SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"Exactly
 what do these statements mean Lance?   "The mouthpiece + 
constriction resonance". "That all tube resonances react accordingly""

I find it more and more difficult to take you seriously John.  How can you pretend, after all the discussion and references in this forum alone, in which you have actively taken part, that you don't know what the resonance of the mouthpiece + constriction is.  Shall I quote you in previous posts referring to it knowingly?  Further, you know very well that tube resonances react to changes in tube dimensions.  I have no time or energy to waste on your intentionally diversionary dramaticism.  If you continue in this fashion, I will just delete your posts without reading them.

"Only the "reed 
closed" volume is static  The EMV volume of a completed cycle then, is 
the average of the "reed open" volumes, averaged with itself and the 
static "reed closed" volume.  As the "reed open" volume is always being 
averaged our perception of the completed cycle, the pitch does not 
change.  

This fails to address the issue that I 
raised that as the mouthpiece equivalent volume goes from the value of 
the average of the open and closed volumes (even if the open volume is 
itself an average) to the constant open volume there will be an abrupt 
change in the mouthpiece equivalent volume which in turn will create an 
abrupt change in the pitch."

You are not thinking. Once the reed starts to close and the minimal volume becomes static, any increase in dynamic level will cause a gradual, dynamic-dependent increase in the "reed open" average within the cycle, and the additive nature of the cycle and our perception, will reduce the effect of any gradual increase by 1/2.   The effect of shifting harmonic resonance peaks within the regime will affect intonation as well.  Regardless, a slight flattening pitch shift is characteristic of louder dynamic level playing, other factors remaining constant.  A weak reed plays flatter than a stiff reed for the same reason.  

"Even if you are 
correct in your first (as yet unproven) assumption "

The theory is proven, by the 100%, universally valid, algebraic formula which applies, and substantiated by the indisputable results of my test.  I invite you AGAIN to disprove the algebraic formula, or show that it does not apply, or to prove my test invalid.  I doubt that you will try, as to do so, you will have to then acknowledge my results and logic.  You prefer to blatantly 
disregard the facts altogether or to play "confused".   

"...your second assumption that
 the MEV is dependent upon the ratio of the opening and closing time of 
the reed is completely false."

I reject your semantic fabrication.  I made no such assumption.  Benade's MEV is strictly based upon the average of the extreme volumes within the cycle.  As I clearly stated in my last post, Benade does not include time domain considerations.  My analysis does, as I consider them important.

"It is true that the ratio between the closing 
and opening time of the reed is equal to the ratio of the truncation 
(length of missing cone) to the tube length (to the first open 
tonehole).  What you are missing completely is the fact that it is the 
length of the tube past the end of the neck that is changing to alter 
the ratio of truncation-- -not the length/volume of the missing cone or 
its substitution."

Again, this is your fabrication.  I stated no such thing.  The resonance of the mouthpiece + constriction, which determines the truncation ratio when compared to the changing tube length, remains constant, as it exists only in the "reed closed" phase, during which the mouthpiece volume is static (the reed actually flexes inward, into the chamber some, but we'll disregard that for now).

You are 0 for 5 there.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Why is knowing the Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range important, if we already know Benade's Equivalent Mouthpiece Volume?

Because, it is at the extreme volumes of each phase within the wave-form's cycle, that the effects of too much or too little volume will first become apparent.  It is there that the regime will break down, long before the effects of the average EMV cause any difficulty at all. 

Why are time domain considerations important?  It's just me, but I'd rather my mouthpiece/reed combination functioned with the horn fantastically for 85% of the time, and OK 15% of the time, as opposed to just average for 50% of the time and average for the other 50%.   If I play bari or bass sax, I'm probably going to me more concerned about how my horn responds in the low end than at the high end, and here time domain considerations will make a big difference, whereas Benade's EMV tells you nothing.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Here's an interesting tidbit from an early engineering report of Benade's for Conn:

The simplest glance at the impedance equation of a resonant system shows that raising the natural frequency increases the magnitude of the apparent stiffness when it is measured below the resonant frequency. This leads to the consequence that raising the frequency of the isolated reed gives rise
 to a sharpening of the pitch of the reed-pipe system, but only asymptotically toward the natural frequency of the pipe alone. From this we see that the properties (such as uniformity of scale, etc.) of the bore of a woodwind, and of its holes may best be studied in the laboratory by asking the
 player to play as sharp as possible, so as to give the pipe the most possible control over the frequency, and to minimize the extent to which the player's own ear and habits can affect his results. The proper tuning desired from an instrument can then be determined with the help of an auxiliary
 set of experiments which show how much flatter than the highest possible pitch a player prefers to play in order to get a good tone and comfortable embouchure
FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Lance wrote:

"Exactly what do these statements mean Lance?   "The mouthpiece + 
constriction resonance". "That all tube resonances react accordingly""

"I find it more and more difficult to take you seriously John.  How can
you pretend, after all the discussion and references in this forum
alone, in which you have actively taken part, that you don't know what
the resonance of the mouthpiece + constriction is.  Shall I quote you in
previous posts referring to it knowingly?  Further, you know very well
that tube resonances react to changes in tube dimensions.  I have no
time or energy to waste on your intentionally diversionary dramaticism. 
If you continue in this fashion, I will just delete your posts without
reading them".

In the interest of clarity, I am going to address each issue in a
separate post.  The first issue is the purpose of my question.  Rather
than "intentionally diversionary dramaticism" as you claim it is an
earnest attempt to understand the meaning of your statement.  You wrote
in an earlier post.

That static minimal volume determines the length of the tube for the
time it takes the wave to go two entire lengths (cylindrical), or
(conical) for the length of time it takes the mouthpiece + constriction
resonance, to go from the truncation, to the theoretical apex, and back,
and all tube resonances react accordingly.

The confusion arises out of the use of the term "mouthpiece +
constriction".  As used by Benade throughout his writings this means the
mouthpiece + the neck.  This way this term is defined affects not only
the point of truncation but  the frequency of the mouthpiece +
constriction resonance as well.

The second part of your statement that is not clear is the second
highlighted statement that "all tube resonances react accordingly".  I
presume that this means the "tube resonances" of a given note "react" to
the shortened tube created by the closing of the reed for the duration
of the cycle that the reed remains closed.

The purpose of this question can best be demonstrated using the
measurements and computations from my
missing cone volume comparison study
<http://jbtsaxmusic.homestead.com/Missing_Cone_Volume_Comparison_Study-R\
evised.pdf>   so please bear with me.

                                      Length          Volume
Missing cone               213.7 mm      8.88 ml
Mouthpiece closed*   105.2 mm     6.87 ml
Difference                    108.5 mm     2.01 ml

*  The mouthpiece closed volume is its measured geometric volume minus
the volume displaced by the the neck.  The length of the "missing cone"
represented by that closed volume is calculated from the volume and the
radius of the mouthpiece shank 7.9 mm using the formula  L = 3V/pi r2

When the written F#1 is sounded on the alto saxophone, at s.s. 345 m/s
the frequency is 220 hz, and the wavelength is 1570 mm.  Since the
wavelength = 2L (twice the length of the instrument)  L = 785 mm

For the duration of the cycle that the mouthpiece is closed at louder
playing levels, the mouthpiece volume
is reduced to 6.87 ml,  and the length of the "missing cone" represented
by that volume is shortened by 108.5 mm.

That means for the duration of the cycle that the mouthpiece is closed
while playing F#1, the tube length of 785 mm is shortened by 108.5 mm 
to 676.5 mm.  Multiplying that by 2 we get the wavelength of 1353.mm.
which falls somewhere between written A1 (1320 mm) and G#1 (1400 mm).

The duration of each cycle that the reed is closed on a conical
instrument is equal to the ratio of the truncation (length of missing
cone) to the length of the tube (from the first open tonehole) to the
apex.  In the case of F#1, the length of the ideal missing cone is 213.7
mm and the length of the tube is 785 mm.
Dividing 213.7 by 785 gives  .27 or 27%.

The period for one cycle is the reciprocal of the frequency, so 1 cycle
at 220 hz is 1/220 second or .0045 second.   Since the reed is closed
for 27% of that time period multiplying .0045 second by .27 we get
.0012 second.

So there won't be any further misunderstanding, my question has been
rephrased to this:

"For the .0012 second that the reed is closed during the cycle playing
F#1 on the alto sax that causes the pitch for that duration to rise by
almost a minor third, what are the reactions of the "tube resonances"
that "act accordingly".

An important corollary question would be:  "What is the significance, if
any, of these reactions of the tube resonances in the thousandth of a
second of the time domain we are discussing"?

Tomorrow I will address the second issue which is the proof and

FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
1. Benade does not contradict himself in the use of Frs or the term
mouthpiece + constriction to mean the mouthpiece plus the neck for the
saxophone.  It is your misuse of the term which makes the communication
of your ideas difficult to understand.

  On page 21 of Acoustical Evolution of Wind Instruments he shows a rough
drawing of the "cavity plus constriction" with the constriction drawn as
a tube extending from the "cavity" and below that the text indicates
that the "constriction" is the sax neck.  When he says that the Frs or
resonant frequency of the cavity plus constriction is the 2nd mode
frequency of the shortest used tube (all the palm keys open) it should
read the 1st mode frequency of that note without the octave key.  The
resonant frequency of the alto sax mouthpiece and neck is its written F2
which is close to this frequency, and the resonant frequency of the
tenor sax mouthpiece on its neck is its written F#2 also close to this
frequency.  Neither of these produce the 2nd mode of that pitch which is
an octave higher.  Benade has never referred to the mouthpiece plus
constriction as the mouthpiece alone with a dimensionless ring inserted
in the shank of the mouthpiece where the end of the neck would be as you
define the term.  In fact, the only way to make that apparatus useful
for acoustic measurements would be to chop off the end at the point of
the "constriction" as you define it.

It would be very difficult to accurately ascertain the resonant
frequency of a mouthpiece alone even if it weren't cut off due to the
fact that without the resonant frequency of an attached tube of some
length to couple with the reed's vibrations, the played pitch can be set
over a wide range of notes with just the slightest embouchure and/or
throat change.

2.  Obviously you do not understand the simple and clear mathematics I
laid out in my response.  You are mistaken.  My results were accurate.  
Please go through the calculations yourself to see that they are in fact
correct.  Please check your own work before calling someone else wrong
in the future.

3.  Both of your responses were  nothing more than attempts to evade the
straightforward questions put to you by changing or obscuring the topic.
Your effort to evade the questions themselves, speaks louder than any
other response you might have offered.

tomorrow I will discuss the proof contained in your study . . .

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Toby,

You are back to your old misconception of mode locking again.  Why?  Did you forget?  Mode locking does not cause resonances to shift at all.  If they are misaligned they are misaligned and they stay that way.  Mode locking causes the regime to shift the frequency at which it forms (and thus the integral fn frequencies), to that point within the half-amplitude bandwidths of the concerned resonances, which provides the most acoustical energy.  The resonances themselves remain unchanged.  The further a resonance's peak is from integral, the less energy it contributes.  If the integral frequency lies outside the half-amplitude bandwidth of the resonance, the resonance can not participate.  

I'll repeat my earlier post: 

Why is knowing the Functional 
Mouthpiece Volume Range important, if we already know Benade's 
Equivalent Mouthpiece Volume?

Because, it is at the extreme 
volumes of each phase within the wave-form's cycle, that the effects of 
too much or too little volume will first become apparent.  It is there 
that the regime will break down, long before the effects of the average 
EMV cause any difficulty at all. 

Why are time domain 
considerations important?  It's just me, but I'd rather my 
mouthpiece/reed combination functioned with the horn fantastically for 
85% of the time, and OK 15% of the time, as opposed to just average for 
50% of the time and average for the other 50%.   If I play bari or bass 
sax, I'm probably going to me more concerned about how my horn responds 
in the low end than at the high end, and here time domain
 considerations will make a big difference, whereas Benade's EMV tells 
you nothing.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
I've forgotten none of this, but what this signifies is that if f1 is present, the frequencies of the higher harmonics are not going to be affected by the timing of the wave, as determined by your shifting mpc volume.

I must admit that I have not followed well the lengthy debate on this subject, because it seems to me totally irrelevant to anything having to do with playing; more an exercise in mental masturbation than anything else. 

You say:


"Because, it is at the extreme  volumes of each phase within the wave-form's cycle, that the effects of  too much or too little volume will first become apparent.  It is there  that the regime will break down, long before the effects of the average  EMV cause any difficulty at all. 

Why are time domain  considerations important?  It's just me, but I'd rather my  mouthpiece/reed combination functioned with the horn fantastically for  85% of the time, and OK 15% of the time, as opposed to just average for  50% of the time and average for the other 50%.   If I play bari or bass 
 sax, I'm probably going to me more concerned about how my horn responds  in the low end than at the high end, and here time domain  considerations will make a big difference, whereas Benade's EMV tells  you nothing."

I am skeptical that this is true. How will the regime break down? Even, arguendo, that the f1 frequency is changed somewhat by it's second cycle up the tube, due to differences in the Veff, what does that have to do with the price of eggs? Mode locking guarantees that the harmonics will march
 right along, whether they further change the regime or not. Yes, this may change in f2, but so what?

Since the sound does not warble, we know that the wave timing is determined by some average of the reed open and reed closed volume. It varies somewhat by strength and embouchure, but this means that the only parameter that is fixed is the mpc volume. So what is your plan? Are you going to vary
 the MEV for each reed and embouchure position?

I hope you realize the import of that Benade quote I posted. The horn must first be adjusted at the smallest MEV, so as to give the truest representation of the tube resonances, and then it must be readjusted at the player's preferred embouchure, because the frequency varies asymptotically towards
 the tube resonances as you progressively pinch up on the reed, but that is not the way you play. All this minute measurement of moment-to-moment MEV divided by the open/closed time of the reed at different frequencies ......

Your theory that the mouthpiece equivalent volume is the average of the

study.

In the study you:
- measured the mouthpiece geometric volume,
-measured a pitch played on the mouthpiece alone
-computed the length of a cylinder with that diameter with that resonant
frequency
-computed the volume of said cylinder
-designated the volume of that cylinder the "average" mouthpiece
equivalent volume
-estimated the maximum mouthpiece volume from the above figures

In other words, you took your assumption that the equivalent mouthpiece
volume was the average  of the closed and maximum open volumes, and
created a theoretical maximum open mouthpiece volume to validate your
assumption.  This is not proof by any stretch of the imagination.  Not
until you can find a way to accurately measure what you call the maximum
mouthpiece opening under actual playing conditions will you be able to

required to match that of the ideal missing cone at low Bb and gradually
diminishes as one plays up the scale.

Your text reads:  "If time domain considerations are added, the
effective mouthpiece volume of this mouthpiece, as per Benade's
cylindrical tube test, will be optimal while playing F3, but will
increase proportional to tube length.

As written by Nederveen, the opening and closing time of a reed on a
conical instrument is approximately equal to the ratio of truncation to
tube length.  This means that as the tube gets longer the closed reed
time in the cycle becomes shorter and shorter.

You are calling my statement "that your assumption is that the MEV is
dependent upon the ratio of the opening and closing time of the reed"  a
semantic fabrication, when it is absolutely clear to anyone who reads
this page of your study that this is exactly what you are showing in
both your graph and your text.  My question is do you even know what
your own study says?

"It is true that the ratio between the closing and opening time of the
reed is equal to the ratio of the truncation
  (length of missing cone) to the tube length (to the first open tone
hole).  What you are missing completely is the fact that it is the 
length of the tube past the end of the neck that is changing to alter
the ratio of truncation---not the length/volume of the missing cone or
its substitution."
Lance wrote:

Again, this is your fabrication.  I stated no such thing.  The resonance
of the mouthpiece + constriction, which determines the truncation ratio
when compared to the changing tube length, remains constant, as it
exists only in the "reed closed" phase, during which the mouthpiece
volume is static (the reed actually flexes inward, into the chamber
some, but we'll disregard that for now).

Ok, Lance.  I had misinterpreted your reasons for claiming that the
"mouthpiece equivalent volume" increases and decreases depending upon
the tube length.  You are still wrong, but I was thinking you were wrong
for the wrong reason.  Now I understand the correct reason you were
wrong.  Please excuse my mistake.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Wrong again, John.  I reject your fabrication.  The assumptions are entirely yours.  Keep them.  My test dealt with actual measurements and accurate conversions only.  Here is my procedure:

I predicted that, due to the additive nature of wave-forms, the duration, length, and equivalent volume of the complete cycle, would be the exact average of the "reed closed" and "reed open" phases.

1.  -recorded the wave-form of the mouthpiece played alone.
2.  -accurately measured the duration of the complete cycle and each phase of the wave-form against the o-scope grid.
3.  -calculated the length of the complete cycle and each phase.
4.  -calculated the equivalent cylindrical tube volumes for each length, based upon the 17mm shank diameter.

Analysis of all data derived directly from the actual wave-form showed that the volume of the tube with the complete wave-form length, was the EXACT average of the tubes with the "reed open" and "reed closed" phases, exactly as predicted and as it should be, according to the additive characteristics of wave-forms and the applicable algebraic formula for averaging, which will give the same results for any mouthpiece, for every similar test, for as many tests as anyone would care to make.  

Later, the water measurement of my mouthpiece (closed reed) was exactly the same as that calculated from the "reed closed" phase duration - to within .00x ml.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"  I had misinterpreted your reasons for claiming that the "mouthpiece 
equivalent volume" increases and decreases depending upon the tube 
length."

It is not wrong.  It is EMV with time domain considerations.  Everything changes if you consider time as part of the formula, which it is.  It is more complete and more accurate than Benade's EMV as it includes the characteristics of the conical air column.  You certainly don't need to include time in your EMV analysis if you are happy applying static cylindrical air column characteristics to explain those dynamically changing characteristics of a  conical tube.  I, personally, am not.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"If, in reality, the mouthpiece equivalent volume so 
poorly matches the volume of the missing cone until the notes approach 
high F, no one would be able to play any saxophone in tune.  Think about
 it.  You are once again defending a proposition that defies the bounds 
of logic and reason."

Only your logic and your reason, because they are based upon an incomplete conception of the reality of the case.   Here is time I will never get back:  

On the same mouthpiece, same horn, same amount of neck inserted, same player, same fingered F3 with a 50/50 "open/closed" reed cycle, a weak read plays flatter than a stiff reed, because it opens further, during the "reed open" phase of the cycle - the equivalent volume of that phase is larger, while the "reed closed"  volumes are equal. 

 On a fingered low Bb, the weak reed will play proportionally even flatter still, than the stiff reed, because:

1. the reed opens further and the equivalent volume is larger, and
2. the larger volume is maintained for a longer period of time per cycle.

Do the math.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
The truncation ratio varies as the ratio of the diameter of the two ends of the conic frustum (assuming a constant cone angle). It varies according to the note being played. It has nothing at all to do with the mpc volume, the smaller end always being the end of the neck.

Toby

-measured a pitch played on the mouthpiece alone 
-computed the length of a cylinder with that diameter with that resonant frequency

-designated the volume of that cylinder the "average" mouthpiece equivalent volume
-estimated the maximum mouthpiece volume from the above figures

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Toby,

The mouthpiece + constriction resonance which re-opens the reed (and thus determines truncation ratio, de facto) occurs in the "reed closed" phase of the wave-form cycle (first part of the 2nd length of the tube).  The length of that phase is entirely dependent on the volume of the mouthpiece.  As the reed is closed, that phase (for that mouthpiece + reed) will always be the same length, no matter what note is played.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
The literature is pretty clear on this point: If the mpc volume reasonably matches that of the missing apex, that takes care of notes up until the highs (IIRC as long as the wavelength is more than 1/4 the length of the truncation). Past that you want the Helmholtz resonance of the mpc to mimic
 that of the missing part of the cone. This is supposed to give a reasonable match from bottom to top, and this is why I feel that this debate is a waste of time. Of course it is your time and you are free to do as you wish with it.

Toby

 Again, this is your fabrication.  I stated no such thing.  The resonance of the mouthpiece + constriction, which determines the truncation ratio when compared to the changing tube length, remains constant, as it exists only in the "reed closed" phase, during which the mouthpiece volume  is static

--- On Mon, 4/12/10, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Monday, April 12, 2010, 3:55 AM







 



  


    
      
      
      Where do you get the information that the reed opens further for lower notes?



MartinMods <lancelotburt@ yahoo.com> wrote:                                      "If, in reality, the mouthpiece equivalent volume so  poorly matches the volume of the missing cone until the notes approach  high F, no one
 would be able to play any saxophone in tune.  Think about  it.  You are once again defending a proposition that defies the bounds  of logic and reason."



Only your logic and your reason, because they are based upon an incomplete conception of the reality of the case.   Here is time I will never get back:  



On the same mouthpiece, same horn, same amount of neck inserted, same player, same fingered F3 with a 50/50 "open/closed" reed cycle, a weak read plays flatter than a stiff reed, because it opens further, during the "reed open" phase of the cycle - the equivalent volume of that phase is larger,
 while the "reed closed"  volumes are equal. 



 On a fingered low Bb, the weak reed will play proportionally even flatter still, than the stiff reed, because:



1. the reed opens further and the equivalent volume is larger, and

2. the larger volume is maintained for a longer period of time per cycle.



Do the math.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
The truncation ratio varies with the length of the tube.  The length of the mouthpiece + constriction resonance, does not change.  You are misreading stuff today.

--- On Mon, 4/12/10, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>

To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Monday, April 12, 2010, 4:01 AM







 



  


    
      
      
      Lance, this cannot be correct, since the truncation ratio varies with the note being played. Joe Wolfe was very clear on this point.



Toby



MartinMods <lancelotburt@ yahoo.com> wrote:                                      Toby,



The mouthpiece + constriction resonance which re-opens the reed (and thus determines truncation ratio, de facto) occurs in the "reed closed" phase of the wave-form cycle (first part of the 2nd length of the tube).  The length of that phase is entirely dependent on the volume of the
 mouthpiece.  As the reed is closed, that phase (for that mouthpiece + reed) will always be the same length, no matter what note is played.



--- On Mon, 4/12/10, kymarto123@ybb. ne.jp <kymarto123@ybb. ne.jp> wrote:



From: kymarto123@ybb. ne.jp <kymarto123@ybb. ne.jp>

Subject: Re: [MouthpieceWork2_ Acoustics] Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test

To: MouthpieceWork2_ Acoustics@ yahoogroups. com

Date: Monday, April 12,  2010, 3:20 AM



                                    The truncation ratio varies as the ratio of the diameter of the two ends of the conic frustum (assuming a constant cone angle). It varies according to the note being played. It has nothing at all
 to do with the mpc volume, the smaller end always being the end of the neck.

 

 Toby

 

 John <jtalcott47@msn. com> wrote:                                         Lance wrote:

 

 The theory is proven, by the 100%, universally valid, algebraic formula which applies, and substantiated by the indisputable results of my test.  I invite you AGAIN to disprove the algebraic formula, or show that it does not apply, or to prove my test invalid.  I doubt that you will 
 try, as to do so, you will have to then acknowledge my results and logic.  You prefer to blatantly  disregard the facts altogether or to play "confused".   

 

 Your theory that the mouthpiece equivalent volume is the average of the closed volume and the maximum open volume has not been proven by your study.  

 

 In the study you:

 - measured the mouthpiece geometric volume, 

 -measured a pitch played on the mouthpiece alone 

 -computed the length of a cylinder with that diameter with that resonant frequency

 -computed the volume of said cylinder

 -designated the volume of that cylinder the "average" mouthpiece equivalent volume

 -estimated the maximum mouthpiece volume from the above figures

 

 In other words, you took your assumption that the equivalent mouthpiece volume was the average  of the closed and maximum open volumes, and created a theoretical maximum open mouthpiece volume to validate your assumption.  This is not proof by any stretch of 
 the imagination.  Not until you can find a way to accurately measure what you call the maximum mouthpiece opening under actual playing conditions will you be able to legitimately claim that have proven your theory.  Until then it is just a hypothesis based upon an assumption.

 

 ...your second assumption that the MEV is dependent upon the ratio of the opening and closing time of the reed is completely false."

 Lance wrote:

  

 I reject your semantic fabrication.  I made no such assumption.  Benade's MEV is strictly based upon the average of the extreme volumes within the cycle.  As I clearly stated in my last post, Benade does not include time domain considerations.  My analysis 
 does, as I consider them important.

 

 Lance look at your study at    http://www.martinmo ds.com/cylcon. htm 

 On page 2 there is a chart which clearly shows that according to your "theory" the equivalent mouthpiece volume "EMV"  is greater than that required to match that of the ideal missing cone at low Bb and gradually diminishes as one plays up the scale.

 

 Your text reads:  "If time domain considerations are added, the effective mouthpiece volume of this mouthpiece, as per Benade's cylindrical tube test, will be optimal while playing F3, but will increase proportional to tube length.

 

 As written by Nederveen, the opening and closing time of a reed on a conical instrument is approximately equal to the ratio of truncation to tube length.  This means that as the tube gets longer the closed reed time in the cycle becomes shorter and shorter.

 

 You are calling my statement "that your assumption is that the MEV is dependent upon the ratio of the opening and closing time of the reed"  a semantic fabrication, when it is absolutely clear to anyone who reads this page of your study that this is exactly what you are showing in 
 both your graph and your text.  My question is do you even know what your own study says?

 

 "It is true that the ratio between the closing and opening time of the reed is equal to the ratio of the truncation 

  (length of missing cone) to the tube length (to the first open tone hole).  What you are missing completely is the fact that it is the  length of the tube past the end of the neck that is changing to alter the ratio of truncation-- -not the length/volume of the missing cone or 
 its substitution. "

      The literature is pretty clear on this point: If the mpc volume reasonably matches that of the missing apex, that takes care of notes up until the highs (IIRC as long as the wavelength is more than 1/4 the length of the truncation). Past that you want the Helmholtz resonance of the mpc to mimic
 that of the missing part of the cone. This is supposed to give a reasonable match from bottom to top, and this is why I feel that this debate is a waste of time. Of course it is your time and you are free to do as you wish with it.

FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test


Lance, I have done the math and the playing tests for over 40 years. The saxophone mouthpiece does not become a mismatch for the volume of the missing cone as one descends the scale to low Bb.  Your premise that the F3 is the perfect volume match is based upon your misunderstanding of Benade's writings.  You have based your whole theory on a false premise and you are just too hard headed to admit it.  You would rather be right than reasonable or logical.

Your study indicates the the maximum open mouthpiece volume to be 2.84 ml larger than the average.  Furthermore you claim that on low Bb the reed is open 86% of the time and closed 14% of the time.  With your proportional averaging then the closed mouthpiece volume of 19.97 ml contributes 14% of the volume or 2.8ml and the maximum open volume of 25.65 ml contributes 86% of the volume or 22.06 ml of the volume for a total of MEV of 24.86 ml which is 2 ml greater than the average EMV which presumably matches that of the missing cone.  This would automatically produce a low Bb that is nearly 40 Cents flat on an instrument that is otherwise built well in tune.  That has not been my experience with saxophone mouthpieces on good instruments for over 40 years.  You might want to reconsider.

--- In MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:
>
> Wrong again, John.  I reject your fabrication.  The assumptions are entirely yours.  Keep them.  My test dealt with actual measurements and accurate conversions only.  Here is my procedure:
> 
> I predicted that, due to the additive nature of wave-forms, the duration, length, and equivalent volume of the complete cycle, would be the exact average of the "reed closed" and "reed open" phases.
> 
> 1.  -recorded the wave-form of the mouthpiece played alone.
> 2.  -accurately measured the duration of the complete cycle and each phase of the wave-form against the o-scope grid.
> 3.  -calculated the length of the complete cycle and each phase.
> 4.  -calculated the equivalent cylindrical tube volumes for each length, based upon the 17mm shank diameter.
> 
> Analysis of all data derived directly from the actual wave-form showed that the volume of the tube with the complete wave-form length, was the EXACT average of the tubes with the "reed open" and "reed closed" phases, exactly as predicted and as it should be, according to the additive characteristics of wave-forms and the applicable algebraic formula for averaging, which will give the same results for any mouthpiece, for every similar test, for as many tests as anyone would care to make.  
> 
> Later, the water measurement of my mouthpiece (closed reed) was exactly the same as that calculated from the "reed closed" phase duration - to within .00x ml.
>

      Now that you mention it Lance, I have been trying to recreate your results using your wav file and my Virtins multi instrument oscilloscope and I have been getting a very different wave form.  I have also bought and downloaded the inexpensive Zelscope software that you used that is much less sophisticated than the Virtins and again there is no similarity when I play your wave file into that either.



Being less experienced than you in dealing with these wave patterns, I have one question.  That is how can you tell exactly which portion of the wave represents the closed reed portion of the waveform, and which portion represents the open reed portion of the waveform?  It seems that your entire study hinges on the millisecond duration of each portion of the wave and that where each starts and ends is of critical importance to the results.



--- In MouthpieceWork2_ Acoustics@ yahoogroups. com, MartinMods <lancelotburt@ ...> wrote:

>

> Wrong again, John.  I reject your fabrication.  The assumptions are entirely yours.  Keep them.  My test dealt with actual measurements and accurate conversions only.  Here is my procedure:

> 

> I predicted that, due to the additive nature of wave-forms, the duration, length, and equivalent volume of the complete cycle, would be the exact average of the "reed closed" and "reed open" phases.

> 

> 1.  -recorded the wave-form of the mouthpiece played alone.

> 2.  -accurately measured the duration of the complete cycle and each phase of the wave-form against the o-scope grid.

> 3.  -calculated the length of the complete cycle and each phase.

> 4.  -calculated the equivalent cylindrical tube volumes for each length, based upon the 17mm shank diameter.

> 

> Analysis of all data derived directly from the actual wave-form showed that the volume of the tube with the complete wave-form length, was the EXACT average of the tubes with the "reed open" and "reed closed" phases, exactly as predicted and as it should be, according to the additive characteristics of wave-forms and the applicable algebraic formula for averaging, which will give the same results for any mouthpiece, for every similar test, for as many tests as anyone would care to make.  

> 

> Later, the water measurement of my mouthpiece (closed reed) was exactly the same as that calculated from the "reed closed" phase duration - to within .00x ml.

>

                                    The literature is pretty clear on this point: If the mpc volume reasonably matches that of the missing apex, that takes care of notes up until the highs (IIRC as long as the wavelength is more
 than 1/4 the length of the truncation). Past that you want the Helmholtz resonance of the mpc to mimic  that of the missing part of the cone. This is supposed to give a reasonable match from bottom to top, and this is why I feel that this debate is a waste of time. Of course it is your time and
 you are free to do as you wish with it.

 

 Toby

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
What is really crazy about this whole conversation is the fact that at the low end, even a huge variation in mpc volume hardly affects the intonation, as the tube impedances are very strong with so much air column length. The cone hardly notices that it is missing anything at the top. This is why
 you cannot lip low notes almost at all.

Toby

John <jtalcott47@...> wrote:                                           
 
 Lance, I have done the math and the playing tests for over 40 years. The saxophone mouthpiece does not become a mismatch for the volume of the missing cone as one descends the scale to low Bb.  Your premise that the F3 is the perfect volume match is based upon your misunderstanding of Benade's
 writings.  You have based your whole theory on a false premise and you are just too hard headed to admit it.  You would rather be right than reasonable or logical.
 
 Your study indicates the the maximum open mouthpiece volume to be 2.84 ml larger than the average.  Furthermore you claim that on low Bb the reed is open 86% of the time and closed 14% of the time.  With your proportional averaging then the closed mouthpiece volume of 19.97 ml contributes 14% of
 the volume or 2.8ml and the maximum open volume of 25.65 ml contributes 86% of the volume or 22.06 ml of the volume for a total of MEV of 24.86 ml which is 2 ml greater than the average EMV which presumably matches that of the missing cone.  This would automatically produce a low Bb that is
 nearly 40 Cents flat on an instrument that is otherwise built well in tune.  That has not been my experience with saxophone mouthpieces on good instruments for over 40 years.  You might want to reconsider.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
From Fletcher:

When treatments of the two parts of the system have been set up in the time domain, it is relatively simple to connect them together by requiring consistency of acoustic quantities at the interface. This generally results in an integral equation s which can be solved numerically to give the flow
 waveform and pressure waveform at the player's mouth, the junction between the generator and the resonator, or the open mouth of the horn. The inclusion of quantities at open finger holes involves splitting the horn into sections placed end-to-end, which clearly complicates the computation. These
 final acoustic quantities are then readily converted to a calculated acoustic output.

One of the nice features of this approach is that it leads automatically to a proper treatment of transients, and particularly of the initial transient, since the integration of the equations requires specification of the way in which blowing pressure is applied and then follows the system
 evolution. This allows for the possibility of different stable solutions depending upon the initiating conditions, a feature of wind-instrument behaviour in certain playing ranges.
Perhaps even more significantly, the time-domain approach makes no
assumptions about the nature of the upper partials of the sound. Their
amplitudes and frequencies are determined automatically from the
computed waveforms. It turns out, indeed, that these upper partials are
generally locked into harmonic relationship with the fundamental, since
musical requirements demand this of a successful instrument design, but
inharmonic relations can arise for certain fingerings and playing techniques.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"It turns out, indeed, that 
these upper partials are
generally locked into harmonic 
relationship with the fundamental, since
musical requirements demand this of a successful instrument 
design"

Come on Toby. This is idiotic.  The resonances are locked because Conn said they had to be, otherwise they wouldn't sell any instruments? 

The definition of a partial is an integral relationship between a resonance frequency and the fundamental.  A partial is locked, by definition.  A resonance is not, as it spans a range of frequencies.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
One more from Fletcher on mode-locking and non-linearity:

"In a sustained-tone instrument, a resonator that is substantially linear in its properties, though not necessarily harmonic, is closely coupled to a generator that is itself driven by a steady force--bow motion or air pressure--and controlled by the resulting oscillations so as to contribute to a
 negative mechanical or acoustic resistance. If this generator were strictly linear, then it would set a large number of resonator modes into oscillation at frequencies little different from their natural frequencies, as determined by the reactive part of the generator impedance, and the sound
 would be entirely different from the phase-locked harmonic sounds to which we are accustomed. This possibility, and its consequences for music, has been explored by Sethares using computer-generated sounds, but will not concern us here.

In reality, the generator in a sustained-tone musical instrument is always highly nonlinear in its response, as shown in the examples of Figure 1.  In each case there is a negative resistance region, but the magnitude of this resistance varies over each cycle of the resonator oscillation. In the
 case of a bowed string and a vibrating reed there is a sharp additional nonlinearity at one end of the operating range. As the mathematics in the Appendix shows, this nonlinearity can lead to shifts in the operating frequencies of all the modes until they are locked into precise harmonic
 relationship, giving the familiar sounds and spectra of Western musical instruments.

This fails only when the modes are extremely inharmonic in their frequency relationships, as for example with peculiar non-standard fingerings on woodwind instruments, and the generator is manipulatred so as to reduce its nonlinearity..."

[my emphases]
FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Take it up with Fletcher. His words not mine. My point is that if a lower mode is present, and strong, basically misaligned resonances are going to be pulled into place (or if they are strong, are going to pull the lower resonance somewhat). That being the case, it won't make a bit of difference
 what the reed timing is, since there can only be a single regime of oscillation at one time (generally speaking, see Fletcher). Yes, higher modes can settle into regimes that have different frequencies from the partials in lower modes, but that seems to me, if I understand you correctly, to be
 quite different from what you are saying.

Toby

>  > > It is not wrong.ï¿½ It is EMV with time domain considerations.ï¿½ Everything changes if you consider time as part of the formula, which it is.ï¿½ It is more complete and more accurate than Benade's EMV as it includes the characteristics of the conical air column.ï¿½
>  You certainly don't need to include time in your EMV analysis if you are happy applying static cylindrical air column characteristics to explain those dynamically changing characteristics of aï¿½ conical tube.ï¿½ I, personally, am not.ï¿½

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Fletcher's terminology is screwy and that confuses you.  A mode is not a resonance.  A mode is a single, specific harmonic resonance frequency that has a specific integral relationship to the fundamental, thus we say, 1st mode, 2nd mode, 3rd mode, etc.  A resonance is a wide range of frequencies.  One of the frequencies of the resonance may participate in the regime and become a mode, if the integral frequency lies within the half-amplitude frequency bandwidth of the resonance.  

Go read Benade again.  

--- On Mon, 4/12/10, kymarto123@... <kymarto123@ybb.ne.jp> wrote:

From: kymarto123@... <kymarto123@...>
Subject: Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Date: Monday, April 12, 2010, 8:53 AM







 



  


    
      
      
      Take it up with Fletcher. His words not mine. My point is that if a lower mode is present, and strong, basically misaligned resonances are going to be pulled into place (or if they are strong, are going to pull the lower resonance somewhat). That being the case, it won't make a bit of difference
 what the reed timing is, since there can only be a single regime of oscillation at one time (generally speaking, see Fletcher). Yes, higher modes can settle into regimes that have different frequencies from the partials in lower modes, but that seems to me, if I understand you correctly, to be
 quite different from what you are saying.



Toby



MartinMods <lancelotburt@ yahoo.com> wrote:                                      "It turns out, indeed, that  these upper partials are
generally locked into harmonic  relationship with the fundamental, since
musical requirements demand this of a successful instrument  design"



Come on Toby. This is idiotic.  The resonances are locked because Conn said they had to be, otherwise they wouldn't sell any instruments? 



The definition of a partial is an integral relationship between a resonance frequency and the fundamental.  A partial is locked, by
 definition.  A resonance is not, as it spans a range of frequencies.







                                 


 
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Boys,

I will now, in two parts, give you the formulas which prove that Benade's EMV, calculated on a cylindrical tube, does not effectively apply to a conical air column, without time domain considerations.  Since John has considerable familiarity with mathematics, I trust he will have no problems with them.  First:

The Cylindrical Tube Wave-form Cycle

This formula describes the additive nature of the (fundamental) wave-form cycle of a sax mouthpiece on a cylindrical tube.  It is universally applicable for any mouthpiece:

X = + phase: duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick.
Y = - phase: duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick.
Z = completed cycle: averaged, duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick, of X and Y.

The Formula:

X/2 + Y/2 = Z

How do we get this formula?  The wave-form consists of 2 phases, X is one of 2, or X/2 and Y is one of 2 or Y/2.  Add them together and you get Z. Simple.

Given any mouthpiece, some tape, some water, and a cylindrical tube, we can easily find the actual closed, hard-walled volume, and Benade's Equivalent Volume, for the mouthpiece.  We'll use John's EMV and MCV test results.  We have these 2 known quantities:

Reed Closed Volume: 6.87 ml.  We make this Y
EMV: 9.13 ml.  This is Z
The Reed Open volume is unknown. We make this X

So we have:

X/2 + 6.87/2 = 9.13

Multiply both sides by 2:

X + 6.87 = 18.26

Subtract 6.87 from both sides:

X = 11.39

The "Reed Open" phase is 11.39 ml, but, we realize that the "Reed Open" volume can not possibly be a static 11.39 ml.  It varies between an infinitely small amount more than the "Reed Closed" volume, (so we'll just use the "Reed Closed" volume for it's minimum value) and an unknown maximum volume, during each "Reed Open" phase.  We can apply our formula to this phase to get the maximum volume amount:

x = unknown maximum value
y = known minimum value (Reed Closed): 6.87 ml
z = completed phase average value: 11.39 ml

x/2 + y/2 = z

or,

x/2 + 6.87/2 = 11.39

multiply both sides by 2:

x + 6.87 = 22.78

subtract 6.87 from both sides:

x = 15.91

This gives us a complete (cylindrical tube) Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range profile ( my earlier diagram had 1 math mistake for the Reed Open max volume: 15.91 ml instead of 16. whatever)

Reed Closed Mouthpiece Volume: 6.87 ml
Equivalent Mouthpiece Volume (average of Reed Closed and Reed Open average: 9.13 ml
Reed Open Volume (averaged): 11.39 ml
Reed Open Min Volume (closed): 6.87 ml
Reed Open Max Volume:15.91 ml

You can try this with as many different mouthpieces as you want, start from the measured closed volume or actual wave-form o'scope measurements.  It works every time.  This information will be of use to us, when we analyze the formula for additive wave-forms of conical tubes, in part II.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Part II

This section will prove that Benade's EMV, as calculated on a cylindrical tube, does not effectively apply to the same mouthpiece on a conical tube.


Conical Tube Wave-form Cycle


This formula describes the additive nature of the (fundamental) wave-form cycle of a sax mouthpiece on a conical tube.  It is identical to the first simple formula that was used for cylindrical tube wave-form cycles, but as is necessary for an accurate description of the Y phase, it includes a factor for the truncation ratio.  The formula provides results identical to those of the cylindrical formula, using cylindrical data.  It is universally applicable for any mouthpiece/horn combination:

X = + phase or Reed Open: duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick.
Y = - phase or Reed Closed: duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick.
Z = completed cycle: averaged, duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick, of X and Y.
TR = truncation ratio: missing cone length/1/2 wave-length

The Formula:

X/2 + (X*(1-TR))/2 + (Y*TR)/2 = Z


How do we get this formula?  The wave-form consists of 2 phases, X, Reed Open, is one of 2, or X/2.  The other one of 2
 consists of:

1. Y or Reed Closed, for the length of TIME it takes sound to go the length of the missing cone (reed closed length)/the length of time it takes sound to go the length of the tube (1/2 wavelength), or the truncation ratio, or Y*TR/2, added to,

2. X or Reed Open, for the remainder of the phase 1-TR (If the TR is .25, then the remainder of the phase is 1-.25, or .75.), or (X*(1-TR))/2.

Add them together and you get Z. Simple again.

Once again, given any mouthpiece, some tape, some water, and a cylindrical tube, we can easily find the actual closed, hard-walled volume, and Benade's Equivalent Volume, for the mouthpiece, and then plug that info into this formula.  We'll use John's EMV and MCV test for the played note, F#1, on his SBA alto.  We have these known quantities:

Reed Closed Volume: 6.87 ml.  We make this Y
EMV: 9.13 ml.  This is Z
The Reed Open volume (average): 11.39 We make this X
Missing Cone Length (reed closed volume): The resonance which re-opens the reed exists in the "reed closed" phase of the wave-form cycle exclusively.  The length of a cone with a base radius of 6.3, and a volume of 6.87 ml is: Length = 3000*6.87/Pi*6.3*6.3, Length = 165.29 mm
Wave-length (F#1) 1570 mm
1/2 wave-length: 785 mm
TR: 165.29/785 = .21

So we have:

X/2 + (X*(1-TR))/2 + (Y*TR)/2 = Z

Or:

11.39/2 + (11.39*(1-.21))/2 + (6.87*.21)/2 = Z

Calculate

5.695 + 4.499 + .721 = 10.915 ml

Here are calculations for various notes based on John's SBA data:

John's missing cone length of 213 mm: 5.965 + 0 + 3.435 = 9.13 ml
F3 392.5 mm wave-length:..............5.965 + 0.911 + 2.885 = 9.49 ml
F2 785 mm wave -length :..............5.965 + 3.303 + 1.442 = 10.44 ml
F1 1570 mm wave-length:...............5.965 + 4.499 + 0.721 = 10.915 ml
Bb0 2493 mm wave-length:..............5.965 + 4.954 + 0.446 = 11.096 ml

The purpose of Benade's EMV calculation using a cylindrical tube, is to establish the AVERAGE volume of the "Reed Open" Average and "Reed Closed" phases of the cycle for the mouthpiece.  It does a precise job, as the EMV remains constant over the playing range of a cylindrical tube of varying lengths. The problem with applying Benade's EMV to a saxophone is, the reed compliance volume (the extra volume based upon the way the reed vibrates) of the mouthpiece on a cylindrical tube, bears no resemblance to the reed compliance volume of the same mouthpiece on a conical tube.  The reed vibrates differently on a conical tube.  The lower the note, the larger the average volume of the "Reed Open" and "Reed Closed" phases.  According to John's method, his saxophone requires a mouthpiece with 8.88 ml volume to work optimally, and though he claims his mouthpiece matches that to within 1%, in actuality, according to the way the reed vibrates on a conical tube,
 it varies from an error of 3.8% (too large) on F3, to 17.7% (too large) on low B, with a 12.5% (too large) error in the middle register.

The solution: match the middle register, average volume, using the conical Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range formula, with the missing cone volume of your instrument, for optimal over-all response and intonation.   If playing baritone or bass and the low register is most prevalent, match the low register average.

I will be providing ongoing tests, comparative intonation charts and audio samples for each method, so readers can decide for themselves, what really works best, the numbers that don't line up, or the numbers that do.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Further:  The actual volume min/max range of the mouthpiece + reed does not change from a cylindrical tube to a conical tube.  Only the average of the phases changes.  The midpoint.  That point in the range where the volume is most of the time, between the two extremes.  The ideal volume.  The most characteristic volume of the mouthpiece + reed under those actual playing circumstances.

                                    Part II
 
 This section will prove that Benade's EMV, as calculated on a cylindrical tube, does not effectively apply to the same mouthpiece on a conical tube.
 
 Conical Tube Wave-form Cycle
 
 This formula describes the additive nature of the (fundamental) wave-form cycle of a sax mouthpiece on a conical tube.  It is identical to the first simple formula that was used for cylindrical tube wave-form cycles, but as is necessary for an accurate description of the Y phase, it includes a
 factor for the truncation ratio.  The formula provides results identical to those of the cylindrical formula, using cylindrical data.  It is universally applicable for any mouthpiece/horn combination:
 
 X = + phase or Reed Open: duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick.
 Y = - phase or Reed Closed: duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick.
 Z = completed cycle: averaged, duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick, of X and Y.
 TR = truncation ratio: missing cone length/1/2 wave-length
 
 The Formula:
 
 X/2 + (X*(1-TR))/2 + (Y*TR)/2 = Z
 
 How do we get this formula?  The wave-form consists of 2 phases, X, Reed Open, is one of 2, or X/2.  The other one of 2
  consists of:
 
 1. Y or Reed Closed, for the length of TIME it takes sound to go the length of the missing cone (reed closed length)/the length of time it takes sound to go the length of the tube (1/2 wavelength), or the truncation ratio, or Y*TR/2, added to,
 
 2. X or Reed Open, for the remainder of the phase 1-TR (If the TR is .25, then the remainder of the phase is 1-.25, or .75.), or (X*(1-TR))/2.
 
 Add them together and you get Z. Simple again.
 
 Once again, given any mouthpiece, some tape, some water, and a cylindrical tube, we can easily find the actual closed, hard-walled volume, and Benade's Equivalent Volume, for the mouthpiece, and then plug that info into this formula.  We'll use John's EMV and MCV test for the played note, F#1, on
 his SBA alto.  We have these known quantities:
 
 Reed Closed Volume: 6.87 ml.  We make this Y
 EMV: 9.13 ml.  This is Z
 The Reed Open volume (average): 11.39 We make this X
 Missing Cone Length (reed closed volume): The resonance which re-opens the reed exists in the "reed closed" phase of the wave-form cycle exclusively.  The length of a cone with a base radius of 6.3, and a volume of 6.87 ml is: Length = 3000*6.87/Pi* 6.3*6.3, Length = 165.29 mm
 Wave-length (F#1) 1570 mm
 1/2 wave-length: 785 mm
 TR: 165.29/785 = .21
 
 So we have:
 
 X/2 + (X*(1-TR))/2 + (Y*TR)/2 = Z
 
 Or:
 
 11.39/2 + (11.39*(1-.21) )/2 + (6.87*.21)/2 = Z
 
 Calculate
 
 5.695 + 4.499 + .721 = 10.915 ml
 
 Here are calculations for various notes based on John's SBA data:
 
 John's missing cone length of 213 mm: 5.965 + 0 + 3.435 = 9.13 ml
 F3 392.5 mm wave-length: ......... .....5.965 + 0.911 + 2.885 = 9.49 ml
 F2 785 mm wave -length :........... ...5.965 + 3.303 + 1.442 = 10.44 ml
 F1 1570 mm wave-length: ......... ......5.965 + 4.499 + 0.721 = 10.915 ml
 Bb0 2493 mm wave-length: ......... .....5.965 + 4.954 + 0.446 = 11.096 ml
 
 The purpose of Benade's EMV calculation using a cylindrical tube, is to establish the AVERAGE volume of the "Reed Open" Average and "Reed Closed" phases of the cycle for the mouthpiece.  It does a precise job, as the EMV remains constant over the playing range of a cylindrical tube of varying
 lengths. The problem with applying Benade's EMV to a saxophone is, the reed compliance volume (the extra volume based upon the way the reed vibrates) of the mouthpiece on a cylindrical tube, bears no resemblance to the reed compliance volume of the same mouthpiece on a conical tube.  The reed
 vibrates differently on a conical tube.  The lower the note, the larger the average volume of the "Reed Open" and "Reed Closed" phases.  According to John's method, his saxophone requires a mouthpiece with 8.88 ml volume to work optimally, and though he claims his mouthpiece matches that to
 within 1%, in actuality, according to the way the reed vibrates on a  conical tube,
  it varies from an error of 3.8% (too large) on F3, to 17.7% (too large) on low B, with a 12.5% (too large) error in the middle register.
 
 The solution: match the middle register, average volume, using the conical Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range formula, with the missing cone volume of your instrument, for optimal over-all response and intonation.   If playing baritone or bass and the low register is most prevalent, match the low
 register average.
 
 I will be providing ongoing tests, comparative intonation charts and audio samples for each method, so readers can decide for themselves, what really works best, the numbers that don't line up, or the numbers that do.
