                                    Further:  The actual volume min/max range of the mouthpiece + reed does not change from a cylindrical tube to a conical tube.  Only the average of the phases changes.  The midpoint.  That point in the range where the volume is most of the time, between the two
 extremes.  The ideal volume.  The most characteristic volume of the mouthpiece + reed under those actual playing circumstances.



--- On Tue, 4/13/10, MartinMods <lancelotburt@ yahoo.com> wrote:

From: MartinMods <lancelotburt@ yahoo.com>
Subject: Re: [MouthpieceWork2_ Acoustics] Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
To: MouthpieceWork2_ Acoustics@ yahoogroups. com
Date: Tuesday, April 13, 2010, 2:08 AM

                                    Part II
 
 This section will prove that Benade's EMV, as calculated on a cylindrical tube, does not effectively apply to the same mouthpiece on a conical tube.
 
 Conical Tube Wave-form Cycle
 
 This formula describes the additive nature of the (fundamental) wave-form cycle of a sax mouthpiece on a conical tube.  It is identical to the first simple formula that was used for cylindrical tube wave-form cycles, but as is necessary for an accurate description of the Y phase, it includes a
 factor for the truncation ratio.  The formula provides results identical to those of the cylindrical formula, using cylindrical data.  It is universally applicable for any mouthpiece/horn combination:
 
 X = + phase or Reed Open: duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick.
 Y = - phase or Reed Closed: duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick.
 Z = completed cycle: averaged, duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick, of X and Y.
 TR = truncation ratio: missing cone length/1/2 wave-length
 
 The Formula:
 
 X/2 + (X*(1-TR))/2 + (Y*TR)/2 = Z
 
 How do we get this formula?  The wave-form consists of 2 phases, X, Reed Open, is one of 2, or X/2.  The other one of 2
  consists of:
 
 1. Y or Reed Closed, for the length of TIME it takes sound to go the length of the missing cone (reed closed length)/the length of time it takes sound to go the length of the tube (1/2 wavelength), or the truncation ratio, or Y*TR/2, added to,
 
 2. X or Reed Open, for the remainder of the phase 1-TR (If the TR is .25, then the remainder of the phase is 1-.25, or .75.), or (X*(1-TR))/2.
 
 Add them together and you get Z. Simple again.
 
 Once again, given any mouthpiece, some tape, some water, and a cylindrical tube, we can easily find the actual closed, hard-walled volume, and Benade's Equivalent Volume, for the mouthpiece, and then plug that info into this formula.  We'll use John's EMV and MCV test for the played note, F#1, on
 his SBA alto.  We have these known quantities:
 
 Reed Closed Volume: 6.87 ml.  We make this Y
 EMV: 9.13 ml.  This is Z
 The Reed Open volume (average): 11.39 We make this X
 Missing Cone Length (reed closed volume): The resonance which re-opens the reed exists in the "reed closed" phase of the wave-form cycle exclusively.  The length of a cone with a base radius of 6.3, and a volume of 6.87 ml is: Length = 3000*6.87/Pi* 6.3*6.3, Length = 165.29 mm
 Wave-length (F#1) 1570 mm
 1/2 wave-length: 785 mm
 TR: 165.29/785 = .21
 
 So we have:
 
 X/2 + (X*(1-TR))/2 + (Y*TR)/2 = Z
 
 Or:
 
 11.39/2 + (11.39*(1-.21) )/2 + (6.87*.21)/2 = Z
 
 Calculate
 
 5.695 + 4.499 + .721 = 10.915 ml
 
 Here are calculations for various notes based on John's SBA data:
 
 John's missing cone length of 213 mm: 5.965 + 0 + 3.435 = 9.13 ml
 F3 392.5 mm wave-length: ......... .....5.965 + 0.911 + 2.885 = 9.49 ml
 F2 785 mm wave -length :........... ...5.965 + 3.303 + 1.442 = 10.44 ml
 F1 1570 mm wave-length: ......... ......5.965 + 4.499 + 0.721 = 10.915 ml
 Bb0 2493 mm wave-length: ......... .....5.965 + 4.954 + 0.446 = 11.096 ml
 
 The purpose of Benade's EMV calculation using a cylindrical tube, is to establish the AVERAGE volume of the "Reed Open" Average and "Reed Closed" phases of the cycle for the mouthpiece.  It does a precise job, as the EMV remains constant over the playing range of a cylindrical tube of varying
 lengths. The problem with applying Benade's EMV to a saxophone is, the reed compliance volume (the extra volume based upon the way the reed vibrates) of the mouthpiece on a cylindrical tube, bears no resemblance to the reed compliance volume of the same mouthpiece on a conical tube.  The reed
 vibrates differently on a conical tube.  The lower the note, the larger the average volume of the "Reed Open" and "Reed Closed" phases.  According to John's method, his saxophone requires a mouthpiece with 8.88 ml volume to work optimally, and though he claims his mouthpiece matches that to
 within 1%, in actuality, according to the way the reed vibrates on a  conical tube,
  it varies from an error of 3.8% (too large) on F3, to 17.7% (too large) on low B, with a 12.5% (too large) error in the middle register.
 
 The solution: match the middle register, average volume, using the conical Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range formula, with the missing cone volume of your instrument, for optimal over-all response and intonation.   If playing baritone or bass and the low register is most prevalent, match the low
 register average.
 
 I will be providing ongoing tests, comparative intonation charts and audio samples for each method, so readers can decide for themselves, what really works best, the numbers that don't line up, or the numbers that do.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Your comments are totally unrelated Toby, and frankly, I'm very surprised at how little insight you have into this.  Benade does not use any wave propagation formulas to figure EMV, so why should I?  All things being equal, the simplest solution is usually the best.  The simple formula, X/2 + Y/2 = Z, which you seem to find incomprehensible, is not mine.  I didn't make it up out of the Blue.  It is the known formula for averaging ANYTHING - something we all learned in 6th grade.  If 10 chickens lay eggs, and they all lay a different amount of eggs, if we add all the eggs up and divide by the number of chickens, we get the average number of eggs per chicken.  Same thing here.  Add the volumes of the phases and divide by phases.  That's EXACTLY what Benade's EMV is. That's really mysterious!  FMVR just adds the information that Benade left out.  The conical formula is the exact same thing, though it adds consideration for the fact that the
 duration of the "reed closed" phase is truncation ratio dependent - an absolutely essential element for an accurate averaging of the reality.  

I don't need to run this by anyone.  It is 6th grade math.

                                    Part II

 This formula describes the additive nature of the (fundamental) wave-form cycle of a sax mouthpiece on a conical tube.  It is identical to the first simple formula that was used for cylindrical tube wave-form cycles, but as is necessary for an accurate description of the Y phase, it includes
 a factor for the truncation ratio.  The formula provides results identical to those of the cylindrical formula, using cylindrical data.  It is universally applicable for any mouthpiece/horn combination:

 Y = - phase or Reed Closed: duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick.

 How do we get this formula?  The wave-form consists of 2 phases, X, Reed Open, is one of 2, or X/2.  The other one of 2

  consists of:

 

 1. Y or Reed Closed, for the length of TIME it takes sound to go the length of the missing cone (reed closed length)/the length of time it takes sound to go the length of the tube (1/2 wavelength), or the truncation ratio, or Y*TR/2, added to,

 

 2. X or Reed Open, for the remainder of the phase 1-TR (If the TR is .25, then the remainder of the phase is 1-.25, or .75.), or (X*(1-TR))/2.

 

 Add them together and you get Z. Simple again.

 

 Once again, given any mouthpiece, some tape, some water, and a cylindrical tube, we can easily find the actual closed, hard-walled volume, and Benade's Equivalent Volume, for the mouthpiece, and then plug that info into this formula.  We'll use John's EMV and MCV test for the played note,
 F#1, on his SBA alto.  We have these known quantities:

 EMV: 9.13 ml.  This is Z

 Wave-length (F#1) 1570 mm

 1/2 wave-length: 785 mm

 TR: 165.29/785 = .21

 Or:

 

 11.39/2 + (11.39*(1-.21) )/2 + (6.87*.21)/2 = Z

MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:                                           Your comments are totally unrelated Toby, and frankly, I'm very surprised at how little insight you have into this.  Benade does not use any wave propagation formulas to figure EMV, so why should I?  All things
 being equal, the simplest solution is usually the best.  The simple formula, X/2 + Y/2 = Z, which you seem to find incomprehensible, is not mine.  I didn't make it up out of the Blue.  It is the known formula for averaging ANYTHING - something we all learned in 6th grade.  If 10 chickens lay
 eggs, and they all lay a different amount of eggs, if we add all the eggs up and divide by the number of chickens, we get the average number of eggs per chicken.  Same thing here.  Add the volumes of the phases and divide by phases.   That's EXACTLY what Benade's EMV is. That's really mysterious!
  FMVR just adds the information that Benade left out.  The conical formula is the exact same thing, though it adds consideration for the fact that the duration of the "reed closed" phase is truncation ratio dependent - an absolutely essential element for an accurate averaging of the reality.  

I don't need to run this by anyone.  It is 6th grade math.

 --- On Tue, 4/13/10, MartinMods <lancelotburt@ yahoo.com> wrote:
 
 From: MartinMods <lancelotburt@ yahoo.com>
 Subject: Re: [MouthpieceWork2_ Acoustics] Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
 To: MouthpieceWork2_ Acoustics@ yahoogroups. com
 Date: Tuesday, April 13, 2010, 2:22 AM
 
                                     Further:  The actual volume min/max range of the mouthpiece + reed does not change from a  cylindrical tube to a conical tube.  Only the average of the phases changes.  The midpoint.  That point in the range where the volume is most of the time, between the two
 extremes.  The ideal volume.  The most characteristic volume of the mouthpiece + reed under those  actual playing circumstances.
 
 
 
 --- On Tue, 4/13/10, MartinMods <lancelotburt@ yahoo.com> wrote:
 
 From: MartinMods <lancelotburt@ yahoo.com>
 Subject: Re: [MouthpieceWork2_ Acoustics] Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
 To: MouthpieceWork2_ Acoustics@ yahoogroups. com
 Date: Tuesday, April 13, 2010, 2:08 AM
 
                                     Part II
  
  This section will prove that Benade's EMV, as calculated on a cylindrical tube, does not effectively apply to the same mouthpiece on a conical tube.
  
  Conical Tube Wave-form Cycle
  
  This formula describes the additive nature of the (fundamental) wave-form cycle of a sax mouthpiece on a conical tube.  It is identical to the first simple formula that was used for cylindrical tube wave-form cycles, but as is necessary for an accurate description of the Y phase, it includes  a
 factor for the truncation ratio.  The formula provides results identical to those of the cylindrical formula, using cylindrical data.  It is universally applicable for any mouthpiece/horn combination:
  
  X = + phase or Reed Open: duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick.
  Y = - phase or Reed Closed: duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick.
  Z = completed cycle: averaged, duration, equivalent length, or equivalent volume, take your pick, of X and Y.
  TR = truncation ratio: missing cone length/1/2 wave-length
  
  The Formula:
  
  X/2 + (X*(1-TR))/2 + (Y*TR)/2 = Z
  
  How do we get this formula?  The wave-form consists of 2 phases, X, Reed Open, is one of 2, or X/2.  The other one of 2
   consists of:
  
  1. Y or Reed Closed, for the length of TIME it takes sound to go the length of the missing cone (reed closed length)/the length of time it takes sound to go the length of the tube (1/2 wavelength), or the truncation ratio, or Y*TR/2, added to,
  
  2. X or Reed Open, for the remainder of the phase 1-TR (If the TR is .25, then the remainder of the phase is 1-.25, or .75.), or (X*(1-TR))/2.
  
  Add them together and you get Z. Simple again.
  
  Once again, given any mouthpiece, some tape, some water, and a cylindrical tube, we can easily find the actual closed, hard-walled volume, and Benade's Equivalent Volume, for the mouthpiece, and then plug that info into this formula.  We'll use John's EMV and MCV test for the played note,  F#1,
 on his SBA alto.  We have these known quantities:
  
  Reed Closed Volume: 6.87 ml.  We make this Y
  EMV: 9.13 ml.  This is Z
  The Reed Open volume (average): 11.39 We make this X
  Missing Cone Length (reed closed volume): The resonance which re-opens the reed exists in the "reed closed" phase of the wave-form cycle exclusively.  The length of a cone with a base radius of 6.3, and a volume of 6.87 ml is: Length = 3000*6.87/Pi* 6.3*6.3, Length = 165.29 mm
  Wave-length (F#1) 1570 mm
  1/2 wave-length: 785 mm
  TR: 165.29/785 = .21
  
  So we have:
  
  X/2 + (X*(1-TR))/2 + (Y*TR)/2 = Z
  
  Or:
  
  11.39/2 + (11.39*(1-.21) )/2 + (6.87*.21)/2 = Z
  
  Calculate
  
  5.695 + 4.499 + .721 = 10.915 ml
  
  Here are calculations for various notes based on John's SBA data:
  
  John's missing cone length of 213 mm: 5.965 + 0 + 3.435 = 9.13 ml
  F3 392.5 mm wave-length: ......... .....5.965 + 0.911 + 2.885 = 9.49 ml
  F2 785 mm wave -length :........... ...5.965 + 3.303 + 1.442 = 10.44 ml
  F1 1570 mm wave-length: ......... ......5.965 + 4.499 + 0.721 = 10.915 ml
  Bb0 2493 mm wave-length: ......... .....5.965 + 4.954 + 0.446 = 11.096 ml
  
  The purpose of Benade's EMV calculation using a cylindrical tube, is to establish the AVERAGE volume of the "Reed Open" Average and "Reed Closed" phases of the cycle for the mouthpiece.  It does a precise job, as the EMV remains constant over the playing range of a cylindrical tube of  varying
 lengths. The problem with applying Benade's EMV to a saxophone is, the reed compliance volume (the extra volume based upon the way the reed vibrates) of the mouthpiece on a cylindrical tube, bears no resemblance to the reed compliance volume of the same mouthpiece on a conical tube.   The reed
 vibrates differently on a conical tube.  The lower the note, the larger the average volume of the "Reed Open" and "Reed Closed" phases.  According to John's method, his saxophone requires a mouthpiece with 8.88 ml volume to work optimally, and though he claims his mouthpiece  matches that to
 within 1%, in actuality, according to the way the reed vibrates on a  conical tube,
   it varies from an error of 3.8% (too large) on F3, to 17.7% (too large) on low B, with a 12.5% (too large) error in the middle register.
  
  The solution: match the middle register, average volume, using the conical Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range formula, with the missing cone volume of your instrument, for optimal over-all response and intonation.   If playing baritone or bass and the low register is most prevalent, match the
 low  register average.
  
  I will be providing ongoing tests, comparative intonation charts and audio samples for each method, so readers can decide for themselves, what really works best, the numbers that don't line up, or the numbers that do.

 > > > 
 > 
 > > 
 > 
 > > > I predicted that, due to the additive nature of wave-forms, the duration, length, and equivalent volume of the complete cycle, would be the exact average of the "reed closed" and "reed open" phases.
 > 
 > > 
 > 
 > > > 
 > 
 > > 
 > 
 > > > 1.$B%F$D(B -recorded the wave-form of the mouthpiece played alone.
 >

 > > > 2.$B%F$D(B -accurately measured the duration of the complete cycle and each phase of the wave-form against the o-scope grid.
 > 
 > > 
 > 
 > > > 3.$B%F$D(B -calculated the length of the complete cycle and each phase.
 > 
 > > 
 > 
 > > > 4.$B%F$D(B -calculated the equivalent cylindrical tube volumes for each length, based upon the 17mm shank diameter.
 > 
 > > 
 > 
 > > > 
 > 
 > > 
 > 
 > > > Analysis of all data derived directly from the actual wave-form showed that the volume of the tube with the complete wave-form length, was the EXACT average of the tubes with the "reed open" and "reed closed" phases, exactly as predicted and as it should be, according to the additive
 characteristics of wave-forms and the applicable algebraic formula for averaging, which will give the same results for any mouthpiece, for every similar test, for as many tests as anyone would care to make.$B%F$D(B 
 >

 > > >

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"You are assuming that there are no time-dependent effects but I 
seriously doubt that. Air is not massless. The only thing, I think, that
 saves you is the fact that in the end it won't make any practical 
difference."


The contrary.  I assume that there are time-dependent effects -  A change in mouthpiece volume with a duration of x seconds, will affect any wave traveling toward the mouthpiece, within the range of distance traveled by the speed of sound in those x seconds, and any wave originating from the mouthpiece within those x seconds..  The bulk modulus of air and other considerations would certainly play a roll, however, in determining EMV (averaging the "reed open" and "reed closed" phases of the cycle via measuring the frequency of the cycle), Benade does not include any formulas other than those which calculate the length of a cylinder of a specific volume and diameter, and the time it takes sound to travel that distance.  Analysis of the actual wave-form, show my averaging calculations to be completely accurate, and further, they substantiate Benade's results.  If you accept Benade's method, FMVR should be accurate enough for our purposes.

FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Lance wrote:

" Show me that the fundamental pitch of ANY wave-form, is NOT the
average  of the durations of it's two phases".

Each completed standing wave is 1 cycle.  Each cycle has two phases, a
compression and rarefaction.  The time it takes for 1 cycle (2 phases)
is 1 period.  The number of cycles in one second is the frequency.  The
frequency and period are reciprocals of one another.

From Lance's Study:

Fundamental pitch 858.37 Hz

duration of phase 1 "closed"    .510 ms
duration of phase 2 "open"      .655 ms
                              Total         1.165 ms

                         Average            .5825 ms

Frequency = 1/period              1/.0005825 s  =  1716.74 Hz

1716.74 Hz  does not equal  858.37 Hz   [B-)]

Let's see, what was the second part of Lance's comment?

Ha, ha, ha.  I meant to say actually is, the fundamental pitch is the average of the duration of each of it's two phases x 2, or theoretically extended into complete cycles of themselves, as per my study.  As complete cycles, you can clearly see how the volume of the fundamental is the average of the volumes of the two extended cycles, and thus, the average of the volume of each phase.  My mistake. FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Since you continue to address me after my departure, I'll take that as an invitation to come back.  LOL.

There is no flaw in the averaging theory, only my previous statement.  

The average of the durations/equivalent lengths/equivalent volumes of the two halves (2 phases) of any wave-form, asymmetrical or not, is exactly 50% the total duration, equivalent lenght, or equivalent volume of the complete wave-form cycle.  The average of the asymmetrical halves equals exactly 50% of the whole.  This is the essence of EMV.

FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
The "averaging" in Lance's "study" can be represented as follows:

2A + 2B divided by 2 = C

Where the Complete Cycle is "C", the Closed Phase is "A", and the Open
Phase is "B"

Doesn't that sound a lot like A + B = C ?  Of course it does, since the
duration of the completed wave is actually the sum of the duration of
its separate phases.  The so called "average" found by multiplying each
phase duration by two, adding them and then dividing by 2  is exactly
the same as just adding the duration of each phase with several
unnecessary steps added.

The flaw is not in the mathematical computations themselves, but in the
logic and reasoning behind the application of the results.  Not seeing
the forest for the trees is a kind way to say the same thing.

BTW I obtained the Rockaboy study, but will have to order the Gokhshtein
through library lending since the BYU Library did not have issues of the
Doklady Physics Journal going back that far.

> The average of the durations/equivalent lengths/equivalent volumes of

exactly 50% the total duration, equivalent lenght, or equivalent volume
of the complete wave-form cycle.Â  The average of the asymmetrical
halves equals exactly 50% of the whole.Â  This is the essence of EMV.

phases, a compression and rarefaction.ÃÂ  The time it takes for 1
cycle (2 phases) is 1 period.ÃÂ  The number of cycles in one second
is the frequency.ÃÂ  The frequency and period are reciprocals of
one another.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"The flaw is not in the mathematical computations themselves, but in the 
logic and reasoning behind the application of the results.  Not seeing 
the forest for the trees is a kind way to say the same thing."

The only flaw here is that  A + B = C is a seriously incomplete view of the reality, and insufficient for an understanding of the  nature of wave-form cycles, EMV, and FMVR.  Given the durations, or equivalent lengths, or equivalent volumes of the phases of the wave-form from my study (well use duration), A = 0.51 ms and B = 0.655 ms, John says that all we need to know is that,  0.51 + 0.655 = 1.165 ms, = 858.35 Hz = 402.39 mm = 91.33 ml, but you know what?  We don't know what the EMV is.  The wave travels 4 lengths of the tube in one cycle, so we can't figure it out (EMV) unless we do that thing that John says it so stupid to do - we have to divide.  Oh no!  If we divide 91.33 by 4, then we get the EMV = 22.81 ml.  Tah-Dah!, but we still don't see clearly how the equivalent volumes of each phase (A = 39.98 and B = 51.35) relate to that, since the wave travels 2 lengths of the tube for each phase.  Oh no!  We have to divide by 2 before we can
 see exactly how the difference in volume is affecting each 1/4 wave-length, and then we see that the EMV lies equidistant and between the minimal volume and the maximum (averaged) volume.

So you stick to your A + B = C,  John.  I'll dig a bit deeper, for a complete understanding if you don't mind.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"So you stick to your A + B = C,  John.  I'll dig a bit deeper, for a 
complete understanding if you don't mind."


A + B = C is the additive formula for waveforms.

X/2 + Y/2 = Z is the formula for EMV

These are 2 different things.  For our purposes, the latter is more important.

That's free information.

Averaging is X/2 +Y/2  = average.
FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
The plate of crow becomes more full with each response.  Go to your
Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test and study the chart at the top
of the page and try to determine what relationships exist going from
left to right.

"X/2 + Y/2 = Z is the formula for EMV"

X is the value of the of the mouthpiece volume calculated using the
duration of the open mouthpiece phase times 2  as though it were a
complete wave in itself.

Y is the value of the mouthpiece volume calculated using the duration of
the closed mouthpiece phase times 2 as though it were a complete wave in
itself.

Z is the value of the mouthpiece volume calculated using the duration
(period) of the entire wave cycle.

Another way to represent this equation is X + Y = 2 Z  since X and Y are
the phase durations doubled.

Using small x and small y as the volume representing each phase found in
the wave cycle Z we get the following equation:

x + y = Z  which is still ADDITION.

In the next installment we will look at how Frequency is independent
from the durations of unequal phases in a completed wave cycle.  Stay
tuned (pun intended).

I have to admit John, you can read that chart.  If Sherlock Holmes were here, he would say that we certainly had a firm grasp on the obvious.  He would most likely add, that we had the uncanny ability to repeat insignificant details with great consistency, while ignoring, by some quality of oversight or less complimentary motives, those truly significant, and their implications.

If you bother yourself to consider the text explanation of the chart, you will see, it clearly states, 

"Theoretical
  full cycle info (as if that phase's volume remained constant for the 
entire
  cycle) is obtained from each phase's actual duration and
  provided for comparison.

The theoretical, full cycle projections of the actual phase durations, or 2A + 2B = , are provided merely for your convenience, so you can make a comparison of equivalent full cycles.  It is not part of the proof.  The proof lies in the actual, non-theoretical values of the chart (those that aren't labeled "theoretical) derived directly from real world measurements - the equivalent 1/4 wave length of the complete cycle for that respective phase (ie. 1/2 the actual phase) and equivalent volume.  This information provides us with X/2 + Y/2 = Z, where X = the equivalent volume of the "reed open" phase, Y = the equivalent volume of the "reed closed" phase and Z = the equivalent volume of the mouthpiece - Benade's EMV, that which we are after, or not?  True, Z is 1/2 an entire cycle, so the whole thing is equivalent to your A + B = C, but C does not equal EMV, and C explains nothing about the degree of asymmetry between the 1/4 wave-length tube length of
 a cylindrical bore nor the combined "reed closed"/"reed open" phase of a conical air column, so your whole diatribe, other than being a means to vent your obvious frustration, is meaningless.

A + B = C:  the duration of the phases add up to the duration of the cycle, regardless of how asymmetrical they are.  It is simple addition:

1 + 9 = 10
2 + 8 = 10
3 + 7 = 10
etc.

This is certainly enlightening.

Y = the equivalent volume of the "reed closed" phase and

also be divided by 2.

The definition of "equivalent volume" as used by Benade is given in this
statement [FMA p. 466]   "In light of these facts, we can hope to
represent a reed and its mouthpiece cavity in terms of an equivalent
hard-walled cavity whose shape is chosen to make its acoustical effect
on the natural frequencies of an air column identical with the composite
effect of the actual reed-plus-cavity on the playing frequencies of the
air column.

From Lance's Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test Study.
"This study shows that what A. Benade terms as the "Effective Mouthpiece
Volume" of a saxophone mouthpiece on a cylindrical tube is actually the
exact average of the actual phase-dependent "reed open" and "reed
closed" volumes of the mouthpiece for each cycle of the waveform".

He calls it the "exact average".  It is  actually  the "sum" of the
average phase volumes as determined by his time domain calculations. 
This is significant in the fact that since the "additive nature of
(portions of ) wave forms" applies, then the resulting frequency will be
derived from the period of one complete wave cycle.  This is true
whether the phases of the wave cycle have a time domain ratio of 1:1, 2
: 1, 3 : 1, 3 :2, etc.

Summary
The frequency of a woodwind instrument is based upon the sum of the
durations of the phases of its cycle, not the average of those
durations.  It follows then that the ratio of the phases of a complete
waveform and any information derived from that ratio such as frequency,
wavelength, volume of a cylinder producing that frequency has no effect
upon the frequency.

This is the "sum" of my argument Lance.  (pun intended)

SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"Right now I am going to the local university to make copies of the 
Rockaboy and Gokhshtein studies that were referenced by Nederveen.  I 
have already found the one by Dalmont et.al. It is fantastic!   It is unfortunate that there isn't more of a spirit of cooperation on 
this forum that might prompt me to share this research with others."

Ha, ha, ha.  If your insight into previous studies is any indication of what you could possibly hope to deny us by your refusal to report on these new documents, we stand to lose nothing and save a lot of time. 

Example #1:

"This information provides us with  X/2 + Y/2 = Z, 

Clearly
 a problem in Addition.  I thought your study was about "averaging"."

Really?  You are kidding right?  Averaging is comprised of 2 calculations:

1. adding the amounts of factors and 
2. dividing by the number of factors added.  

Let's go back to 6th grade then, since you forgot 6th grade math:

Chicken "Ex" lays X number of eggs.  Chicken "Why" lays Y number of eggs.

If we add the X number of eggs to the Y number of eggs, and divide by 2, we get the average number of eggs laid.

(X + Y)/2 = X/2 + Y/2 = Z or Average of X and Y.  

Example #2:

"X is derived from the 1/4 the wavelength of the "theoretical pitch" of 
the "reed open" phase as though it were a complete cycle.  Since the 
phase is actually ½ the theoretical complete cycle, that volume must be 
divided by 2."

 Totally wrong.  Data derived from theoretical data, in any quantity, is always labeled "Theoretical". Actual measurement data converted to other quantities, is termed "equivalent".  

X is the actual measured phase duration.  Y is the actual measured phase duration.  

Example #3:


"True, Z is 1/2 an entire cycle, 

Actually Z is an entire 
cycle..."

Totally wrong.  For the formula: X/2 + Y/2 + Z,  When X = the "reed open" duration or equivalent quantity, and Y = the "reed closed" duration or equivalent quantity, then X/2 = 1/4 wave-length, and Y/2 = 1/4 wave-length and Z equals the sum of the two, 1/4 wave-lengths, which is the average of the two (1/2 wavelength long) phases.  Z is exactly 50% of an entire cycle, which is directly related to EMV and the added reed compliance.
FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Benade's numbered statements are a clear way to communicate.

1. Your value X is based upon the duration of the "open reed"
    wave phase doubled.  Your value Y is based upon the "closed reed"
    wave phase doubled.

2. Your theoretical pitch derived from wave phase is based upon those
    wave phases doubled as though each were a complete wave in itself.

3. Your theoretical wavelengths are based upon those wave phases
    doubled as well.

4. Your 1/4 cycle length is based upon those wave phases doubled.

5. Your mouthpiece equivalent volumes are calculated using the 1/4
    cycle lengths based upon those wave phases doubled.

6. When you use the formula  X/2 +  Y/2  =  Z  you are neglecting to
    realize that the values of X and Y are based upon the phase
    (approx.1/2 wave) values that have been doubled throughout.

7. Dividing each by 2 and then adding simply returns each to the
    initial phase value prior to being doubled.

8. Therefore in effect you are using addition---not averaging.


x = closed phase duration
y = open phase duration
Z = duration of complete wave cycle

x + y = Z

2x = X  - duration of phase as if it were a complete wave cycle by
itself.
2y = Y  - duration of phase as if it were a complete wave cycle by
itself.

Therefore:

X/2  +  Y/2  =  Z

I hope this is clear now.  I am tiring of this discussion of rudimentary
arithmetic.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
First realize that the reed is only open once during each cycle, as the reed is only closed once during each cycle.

"1. Your value X is based upon the duration of the "open reed" 
   
wave phase doubled.  Your value Y is
 based upon the "closed reed"
   wave phase doubled."

Wrong.  Using my test info, X is based upon the actual duration of the "Reed Open" phase, 0.655 ms, during which the wave travels 2 lengths of the tube.  Y is based upon the actual duration of the "Reed Closed" Phase, 0.51ms, during which the wave travels 2 lengths of the tube.  NOTHING is doubled for proof calculations.

"2. Your theoretical pitch derived from wave phase is based upon those 
  
 wave phases doubled as though each 
were a complete wave in itself."

This is totally irrelevant, since the theoretical numbers are not used for the proof.  They are provided for a comparison only.

"3. Your theoretical wavelengths are based upon those wave phases
   doubled as well."

This is totally irrelevant, since the theoretical numbers are 
not used for the proof.  They are provided for a comparison only.


"4. Your 1/4 cycle length is based upon those wave phases doubled."

Totally Wrong  The 1/4 cycle length is based upon 1/2  the actual duration of each phase of the actual (not theoretical - thus it is not labeled "theoretical") complete cycle.  Where each phase was X and Y, the 1/4 cycle is X/2 and Y/2.

X = 0.655 ms and Y = 0.51 ms

"5. Your mouthpiece equivalent volumes are calculated using the 1/4
  
 cycle lengths based upon those wave phases doubled."

Wrong.  The equivalent volumes are based upon 1/2 the actual phase durations of the actual cycle ( thus they are not labeled "theoretical"), or X/2 and Y/2

"6. When you use the formula  X/2 +  Y/2  =  Z  you are neglecting to
  
 realize that the values of X and Y are based upon the phase
   
(approx.1/2 wave) values that have been doubled
 throughout."

Wong.  X and Y have never been doubled for any proof calculation.  X = 0.655 ms and it's equivalent quantities.  Y = 0.51ms and it's equivalent quantities.

"7. Dividing each by 2 and then adding simply returns each to the
   
initial phase value prior to being doubled."

Wrong.  X and Y were never double for any proof computation.

FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"4. Your 1/4 cycle length is based upon those wave phases doubled."

Totally Wrong  The 1/4 cycle length is based  upon 1/2  the actual
duration of each phase of the actual (not  theoretical - thus it is not
labeled "theoretical") complete cycle.   Where each phase was X and Y,
the 1/4 cycle is X/2 and Y/2.

Sorry  Lance.  What you are missing is the fact that regardless of
whether you  are treating the open reed phase as a half cycle and saying
X/2 equals  1/4 wavelength or that the open reed phase is a whole cycle
and X/4  equals 1/4 wavelength, you are still treating your  phase as
though it were a complete wave which it is not.

That  means that when you arrive at your 112.99 mm 1/4 wavelength and
its  corresponding 25.65 ml volume, you have still treated the open reed
phase duration of the cycle as though it were a complete wave cycle in 
itself.  Therefore when that value is divided by 2 you are converting it
back to its original phase (not complete wave) value.

X =  0.655 ms and Y = 0.51 ms

Here are the calculations where X  was never double for any proof
computations as you claim

X =  .655 ms = .000655 s

F = 1/.000655 = 1526.72 Hz

Wavelength  = 345000 mm/s / 1526.72 = 225.97 mm

1/4 Wavelength = 225.97 / 4 =  56.49 mm*

Equivalent volume = 3.1416 x 8.5 sq x 56.49 =  12.822 ml

Here are the calculations where X is double for the  computations

X = .655 ms

2X = 1.31 ms

F =  1/.00131 = 763.36 Hz

Wavelength = 345000 mm/s / 763.36 = 451.95  mm

1/4 Wavelength = 451.95 / 4 = 112.99  mm

Equivalent volume = 3.1416 x 17 sq x 112.99 = 25.65  ml

* You can argue that you only  divided by 2 here to get 112.99 mm
instead of 56.49 mm, however that  simply takes you to the X is double
value, does it not?

When the equivalent volumes for the open and  closed reed phases that
have  been calculated as though each were a  complete wave in itself 
are divided by 2---those values revert back to  the volumes that would
be produced by each phase alone. Therefore when  adding those volumes
divided by 2 it is a function of addition and not  averaging.

4  +  10   =  14  the sum of 4 and 10

8/2   +  20/2  =  14  the sum of 4 and 10 also the average  of 8 and 20
which are 4 and 10 doubled.

Please  entertain the idea that you just might be wrong on this one
Lance,  because you are.  I am trying to do you a favor in pointing out
the  flaws in your logic and conclusions, so that you won't waste your 
time  in the future with theories and ideas based on this false
information.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Amazing thought process, but, here are the calculations that I made for my proof:


X = 0.655ms (actual measured duration)
0.000655 * 345400 = 226.23 mm (actual distance traveled by wave during phase)
pi * 8.5 *8.5 * 226.23 = 51.35 ml (actual equivalent volume of 17mm diameter tube 226.23mm long)
X/2 = 25.65 ml (equivalent volume of actual 1/4 "reed open" tube length, 1st averaging calculation)

Y= .0.51 ms (actual measured duration)
0.00051 * 345400 = 176.15 mm (actual distance traveled by wave during 
phase)

pi * 8.5 *8.5 * 176.15 = 39.98 (actual equivalent volume of 17mm 
diameter tube 176.15mm long)

Y/2 = 19.97 ml (equivalent volume of actual 1/4 "reed closed" tube length and actual, water method measured, hard-walled mouthpiece volume, 1st averaging calculation)


25.65 + 19.97 = 45.62 ml (2nd averaging calculation = average of actual 51.35 ml and 39.98 ml phase equivalent volumes, 50% of actual asymmetrical cycle)

45.62 / 2 = 22.81 ml average 1/4 wave-length equivalent volume of actual asymmetrical cycle AND Benade's EMV for my mouthpiece.

So, actually I did make one little error.

X/2 + Y/2 = Z (average cycle value = 50% of asymmetrical wave-form)

Z/2 = average 1/4 wave-length volume = Benade's EMV for my tubeless mouthpiece.  With a tube, we still need to subtract the known tube volume.

This also gives us

22.81 (average 1/4 wl volume) - 19.97 (reed closed volume) = 2.84 ml
25.65 (average reed open volume*) - 22.81 (average 1/4 wl volume) = 2.84

22.81 +/- 2.84 ml: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range (average max)

X/2 (unknown max volume/2) + 19.97/2 (known minimum/2) = 25.65 (average max)

X + 19.97 = 51.3

X = 31.33 ml (actual max reed open volume)

31.33 - 22.81 = 8.52 ml

22.81 ml + 8.52lm/-2.84 ml: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range (actual max)

One could also just go:

X + Y = Z 

Z / 4 = average 1/4 wave-length volume = Benade's EMV for my tubeless 
mouthpiece,
but I can make good use of the range info, and I intend to demonstrate, with the conical tube EMV tests, that cylindrical tube EMV does not apply to conical tubes and that this information is essential to optimally matching a mouthpiece to an instrument.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"31.33 - 22.81 = 8.52 ml

22.81 ml + 8.52lm/-2.84 ml: Functional 
Mouthpiece Volume Range (actual max)"

Correction.  Actually, that is: 

8.52 ml = max open volume
19.97 ml = minimum volume
8.52 - 2.84 = 5.68

22.81 ml +5.68/-2.84 ml = Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range (actual max, cylindrical tube)
FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test


Come on Lance, one little error?  Your entire study was presented as proof beyond a doubt that the mouthpiece volume of the complete wave is the average of the volumes of the separate phases of that wave.  That has turned out to be absolutely false as I have demonstrated time and time again.

That means that the second part of your study that the frequency of a conical instrument is dependent upon the ratio of the reed open and reed closed phases because of their different mouthpiece volumes is false as well.

That is not one little error. That is the entire study that lacks validity.  If there is no averaging that means the whole is the sum of its parts, not the average.  If the equivalent mouthpiece volume is the sum and not the average of the volume calculated from each phase of the wave, it follows that the ratio or proportion of those phases to the entire wave cycle has no effect upon the resulting frequency.  Said frequency is derived from the sum of the wavelengths of each phase.

You are not fooling anyone but yourself by continuing this line of reasoning based upon your fatally flawed mouthpiece equivalent volume averaging study.  Please admit your mistake and move on.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Dalmont Study
I think it's noteworthy that in analyzing the effects of increased conicity in the upper body (neck) of conical air columns, that Dalmont both diagrammed and explained in words and formula, how the missing cone volume is appropriately based upon the taper of the conical main body.  He did add that the effects of dynamically changing mouthpiece volume, tone-holes, etc., could show that these calculations are not entirely accurate without some compensations, but at no point did he (..and neither did Nederveen in his treatment of the same material) mention that the possibility of calculating the missing cone volume from the increased taper of the top bore section, as the representative for the entire body's requirements, was worthy of consideration .  Perhaps they just missed that......  Anyway, lots more hands-on measurements and analysis need doing.
FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Dalmont Study
Intuitively (meaning without hard data) I feel that the missing apex should be figured (at least for the low notes)  on the main part of the cone, but of course changing the conicity of the top part of the cone would certainly affect the top-tube notes and the upper register: any note where the
 upper cone comprises more than a quarter-wavelength or so. Once the conicity is no longer constant you are in a compromise position in any case. It probably makes the most sense to get the long-tube notes in tune first and then tweak things to try to get the shorter-tube and higher register notes
 in tune with various tricks.

Toby

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)

Of course.  As Dalmont states, the conicity of the upper tube affects the shorter wave-lengths more than the longer wave-lengths. I can't think of a reason not to attempt satisfy both requirements. Ignoring the body which is the core of the instrument, is silly I think.

                                    Intuitively (meaning without hard data) I feel that the missing apex should be figured (at least for the low notes)  on the main part of the cone, but of course changing the conicity of the top part of the cone would certainly affect the top-tube notes and the
 upper register: any note where  the upper cone comprises more than a quarter-wavelength or so. Once the conicity is no longer constant you are in a compromise position in any case. It probably makes the most sense to get the long-tube notes in tune first and then tweak things to try to get the
 shorter-tube and higher register  notes in tune with various tricks.
 
 Toby
 
 MartinMods <lancelotburt@ yahoo.com> wrote:
                                      I think it's noteworthy that in analyzing the effects of  increased conicity in the upper body (neck) of conical air columns, that Dalmont both diagrammed and explained in words and formula, how the missing cone volume is appropriately based upon the taper of
 the conical main body.  He did add that the effects of dynamically changing mouthpiece  volume, tone-holes, etc., could show that these calculations are not entirely accurate without some compensations, but at no point did he (..and neither did Nederveen in his treatment of the same material)
 mention that the possibility of calculating the missing cone volume from the increased  taper of the top bore section, as the representative for the entire body's requirements, was worthy of consideration .  Perhaps they just missed that......  Anyway, lots more hands-on measurements and analysis
 need  doing.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Dalmont Study
Dalmont makes interesting mention of how the degree of harmonicity/inharmonicity affects  the way the resonances of the regime increase in amplitude (as Benade stated - exponentially, compared to f1, until the reed 
begins to close), and the effects of changes in reed compliance on the same.  If we realize that the reed compliance changes with dynamic level (the wider the reed opens, the more reed compliance) and register (the reed compliance varies 2ml over the range of my tenor, lower register compliance is larger than the high register) we can match the volume of our mouthpiece to the horn in such a way as to maximize the results for all registers and dynamic levels.  For low dynamic levels and high register playing, where f1 is really the only active resonance mode, inharmonicity is not such an issue, a mouthpiece with less volume than the ideal will suffice and enable an increasingly better match as the dynamic increases, the higher resonance modes become stronger, and the reed compliance increases, and an ideal match for louder dynamic levels, where the reed closes, and opens it's widest, and resonance amplitude increases become linear.

In this way, the horn's response will be optimized.  Matching the missing cone volume to a static EMV based upon at the middle dynamic level reed compliance, will result in worsening inharmonicity as the dynamic level, resonance amplitude increases become linear.  The horn will tend to choke or back up on the player.

A similar optimization for register can be done for the larger saxophones, on which low end response is so important.

      Definitely, one has to (somehow) take into account any part of the body which participates in forming the air column for a given note.



Toby

 not to attempt satisfy both requirements. Ignoring the body which is the core of the instrument, is silly I think.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Dalmont Study
For lower register playing inharmonicity is (AFAIK) still a big issue, and perhaps even greater for the lowest notes where your 2nd peak is stronger than the first. The 2nd peak is strong throughout the lower register as well. And don't forget that volume affects the tuning very strongly.

Toby

FROM: kwbradbury (Keith Bradbury)
SUBJECT: Re: Dalmont Study
If some clever sax designer determined that results were better when the upper taper (neck) was tapered more than the body, it seams to me that this adjustment would extend to the very tip and include the mouthpiece.  So the mouthpiece volume would be based on the neck taper.  The body taper is not ignored in that the neck taper is determined by it.  Similar, but tweaked.  

But some careful measurements and analysis should answer the question better than conjecture has been doing.

From: MartinMods <lancelotburt@...>
To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
Sent: Tue, April 27, 2010 11:59:42 PM
Subject: Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Dalmont Study

  
Of course.  As Dalmont states, the conicity of the upper tube affects the shorter wave-lengths more than the longer wave-lengths. I can't think of a reason not to attempt satisfy both requirements. Ignoring the body which is the core of the instrument, is silly I think.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Dalmont Study
"For lower register playing inharmonicity is (AFAIK) still a big issue, 
and perhaps even greater for the lowest notes where your 2nd peak is 
stronger than the first.

Right, but everything is a matter of degree.  As Dalmont says, we can manage inharmonicity in the low register easier than in the higher registers, and the wanna-be dominant f2 of low B, for example, can be tamed by making small adjustments in the neck at a node if needed..  

The point is, you can make more out of matching your mf EMV to the missing cone than just using a "middle of the road" setting, so to speak.   Though that might be just fine for most players, there are certainly players that take advantage of it, knowingly or not.  Here we could go back to my old discussion of Pete Christlieb and his 160/whatever Berg.  With a mouthpiece that open and a soft reed, you have a lot of control over a huge range of reed compliance.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Dalmont Study
Coupla things. First, the neck taper anyway changes at the cork, becoming almost cylindrical, if not actually cylindrical, for the last inch or so. Some makers "neck in" to improve the tuning of the upper register as well. The curve of the neck also changes the taper, so there is no continuous
 neck taper to begin with.

Second, since there is no standardization between makers, there is no way for mpc makers to design their mpcs for a specific taper/neck.

I think you will find that mpc volumes are all over the place, and of course saves us is the fact that we can move the mpc on the neck.

Toby

Keith Bradbury <kwbradbury@...> wrote:                                           If some clever sax designer determined that results were better when the upper taper (neck) was tapered more than the body, it seams to me that this adjustment would extend to the very tip and include the
 mouthpiece.  So the mouthpiece volume would be based on the neck taper.  The body taper is not ignored in that the neck taper is determined by it.  Similar, but tweaked.  
 
 But some careful measurements and analysis should answer the question better than conjecture has been doing.

 From: MartinMods <lancelotburt@...>
 To: MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com
 Sent: Tue, April 27, 2010 11:59:42 PM
 Subject: Re: [MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics] Re: Dalmont Study
 
   
 Of course.  As Dalmont states, the conicity of the upper tube affects the shorter wave-lengths more than the longer wave-lengths. I can't think of a reason not to attempt satisfy both requirements. Ignoring the body which is the core of the instrument, is silly I think.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Dalmont Study
"The body taper is not ignored in that the neck taper is determined by it."

...but, if we then bother considering:

1. mouthpiece volume matching is intended to satisfy low frequency resonance requirements - for resonances with long wave-lenghts, equal to up to 2x entire body length.

2. neck taper is intended to improve high frequency resonance alignment - which would be limited to resonance of  wave-lengths shorter than the entire body length.

...we see that, the neck taper is derived from only a few aspects of the body taper.  It does not reflect it's entirety.
FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Dalmont Study
This makes sense to me. 

Toby

MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:                                           "The body taper is not ignored in that the neck taper is determined by it."

...but, if we then bother considering:

1. mouthpiece volume matching is intended to satisfy low frequency resonance requirements - for resonances with long wave-lenghts, equal to up to 2x entire body length.

2. neck taper is intended to improve high frequency resonance alignment - which would be limited to resonance of  wave-lengths shorter than the entire body length.

...we see that, the neck taper is derived from only a few aspects of the body taper.  It does not reflect it's entirety.
FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Dalmont Study
Dalmont does include calculations for the transverse motion of the reed Ur as an integral part of the total input air flow U, so perhaps we can not completely discount Ferron's baffle reflection theory.

FROM: saxgourmet (STEVE GOODSON)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Chuck:
Since you brought it up, I would like to have acoustic benefits of the  
stones explained in terms I can understand. I've examined some of the  
necks with the stone inserts, and the stone itself has very little  
mass. I've played numerous examples of the horns in question from this  
maker and frankly can't tell the difference in stone vs. non-stone  
examples....they look different and that appears to be all. There are  
some acoustic claims being made for these stones, and I would like to  
know if there is anything to back this up other than the  
manufacturer's claims.

We do add weights to specific points of some of our necks. The amount  
of mass we add is significant. We find the addition of mass to  
pressure node points significantly influences the response of specific  
notes. This mass is added directly to the neck tube, not on a "collar"  
at the tenon.

> > 226.23 = 51.35 ml (actual equivalent volume of 17mm diameter tube
> > 226.23mm long)
> > X/2 = 25.65 ml (equivalent volume of actual 1/4 "reed
> > open" tube length, 1st averaging calculation)
> >
> > Y= .0.51 ms (actual
> > measured duration)
> > 0.00051 * 345400 = 176.15 mm (actual distance
> > traveled by wave during
> > phase)
> >
> > pi * 8.5 *8.5 * 176.15 = 39.98 (actual equivalent volume of 17mm
> > diameter tube 176.15mm long)
> >
> > Y/2 = 19.97 ml (equivalent volume of actual 1/4 "reed closed" tube
> > length and actual, water method measured, hard-walled mouthpiece  
> volume,
> > 1st averaging calculation)"
> >
> > Another minor correction here:
> >
> > "tube length" should be "wave-length" (of the complete original  
> cycle, from the respective phase)
> >
>
>
>
FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
This is a very fascinating topic in the area of acoustics---whether or not changes to the outside of the body tube can measurably or perceptively change the vibrating air column inside the instrument.  To date there have been no scientific studies that have found this to be true.  That said, there is lots of anecdotal evidence that some players perceive there is a difference in different metals, finishes, brace locations, etc.

I would like to know the names of the notes on a tenor sax whose "pressure nodes" are at the location of the added brass weights to your specially designed neck.  I would also like you to share with us which specific notes whose "response" is significantly affected by adding mass at these points.


--- In MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com, STEVE GOODSON <saxgourmet@...> wrote:

> Chuck:
> Since you brought it up, I would like to have acoustic benefits of the

> necks with the stone inserts, and the stone itself has very little  
> mass. I've played numerous examples of the horns in question from this  
> maker and frankly can't tell the difference in stone vs. non-stone  
> examples....they look different and that appears to be all. There are  
> some acoustic claims being made for these stones, and I would like to  
> know if there is anything to back this up other than the  
> manufacturer's claims.
>

> of mass we add is significant. We find the addition of mass to  
> pressure node points significantly influences the response of specific  
> notes. This mass is added directly to the neck tube, not on a "collar"  
> at the tenon.

FROM: saxgourmet (STEVE GOODSON)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
I was first introduced to the concept of external weights by Jim  
Schmidt about a dozen years ago. Jim had a couple of horns with  
weights all over the bodies. I didn't play the horns before the  
weights were applied, so I can't speak as to any difference. Jim is a  
REAL mad scientist type, who is not afraid to try new ideas, and I  
always listen to what he has to say and have great faith in his  
findings. He has done some very interesting stuff over the years.

We experimented a great deal with different mass weights and different  
locations and learned some interesting stuff. In truth, some of the  
necks we built up that played the best were simply to ugly to sell.

I've put up a great many neck demo videos on my saxgourmetproducts  
(one word) YouTube site, so anyone who is interested can check them  
out. Insofar as revealing the actual math, I'll pass on that  
opportunity, since I don't wish to do my competitors R&D for them!

I'm currently working on some necks as a joint venture with my friend  
Rheuben Allen. These necks have some pretty large weights which can be  
moved and arranged in different configurations. The different  
configurations give different sounds. You can see some pictures at
http://saxophonethoughts.blogspot.com/  Rheuben has been fooling with  
this concept for about a year, and I've been experimenting with  
different configurations of his product. He and I also have several  
joint ventures underway currently which involve different  
manufacturing techniques for necks which eliminate braces, seams, and  
the like. We're developing a neck that is a continuous, seamless tube,  
from tip to tenon.

FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
With all due respect, no one has asked for "the math" as you call it. 
Your statement in your first post was:

"We find the addition of mass to pressure node points significantly
influences the response of specific notes."

Since you are publicly making this claim, I am simply asking you which
notes "pressure node points" you believe are found in the location of
the tenor sax neck where you have added the three brass ovals and which
specific notes have an improved response.  Believe me that there is no
one on this forum who wants to "steal" your "R&D " and compete with you.
That is absurd.  I am simply asking you to expand upon your claim by
giving some  specifics about the pitches affected by adding mass at
these points.

Any additional insight into how and why adding mass at pressure node
points affects the vibrating air column inside the tube would be most
welcome as well.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test

FROM: saxgourmet (STEVE GOODSON)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Almost no manufacturing companies of which I am aware share  
proprietary technology and research with the public. I see no reason  
our company should be treated any differently. We have stated benefits  
that we have discovered, and that is as far as we see any need to go.  
We are a privately held company, and have no obligation whatsoever to  
public disclosure of confidential information. I see no reason that  
anyone needs to know the particulars of or benefit from our research.  
If we had wished to make the particulars public, we would most likely  
have done so in one of my regular articles on saxophone design which  
appear in each and every issue of The Saxophone Journal.

We believe we have adequately demonstrated the benefits of our design  
through the public publication of numerous product videos. I see no  
reason this should not suffice. This is FAR more information than any  
other manufacturer provides. We do a LOT of experimentation, as anyone  
who has visited our prototype shop will attest. Most of what we try  
does not work, and that is useful information to us. The innovations  
that do prove successful often find their way onto our products which  
we offer for sale. Since research and experimentation comes at a  
significant financial cost to us, we elect not to do it on behalf of  
the competition! I don't get a lot of calls from Peter Ponzol, Oleg  
Garbozov, Karsten Gloger and others saying "Hey, Steve, I just figured  
out something that works. Here are the specifics, you should use it on  
your necks!".

I note with interest that other neck manufacturers do not provide  
specific information on their designs, and see no reasonable reason  
why we should be called upon to do so, particularly in light of the  
fact that we provide infinitely more public information about our  
products than any of our competitors. We state the general principles  
we discover and demonstrate them to the public, which is more than  
anyone else does.

FROM: saxgourmet (STEVE GOODSON)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
We find that a seamless tube resonates better......we're still fooling  
around with different materials and manufacturing techniques, since we  
will be, in the grand scheme of things, producing them in significant  
quantity. The biggest issue we are having with this design currently  
is a high "scrap rate" when using solid silver. We will figure that  
out in time. Sharon and I are going across the Great Waters to the  
West pretty soon in order to spend some time at the various factories  
we deal with and to see if we can figure out some solutions to this  
problem and develop some new products. The factories all have  
equipment we lack at our prototype shop, so the experiments can go  
quicker over there. We'll also have some experienced saxophone  
builders to assist us. When I build things here, I have to do it all  
myself!

> My new neck is a continuous, seamless tube, from tip to body.

>

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
I think that David Monet's approach could be applied to saxophones with positive results, i.e., the use of thick massive construction in his "Rava" trumpet, eliminating as much sympathetic wall vibration as possible.  Wall vibrations will never be in phase with those of the air column, due to the difference in mass.  They can only cause interference.

      We find that a seamless tube resonates better...... we're still fooling around with different materials and manufacturing techniques, since we will be, in the grand scheme of things, producing them in significant quantity. The biggest issue we are having with this design currently is a high "scrap rate" when using solid silver. We will figure that out in time. Sharon and I are going across the Great Waters to the West pretty soon in order to spend some time at the various factories we deal with and to see if we can figure out some solutions to this problem and develop some new products. The factories all have equipment we lack at our prototype shop, so the experiments can go quicker over there. We'll also have some experienced saxophone builders to assist us. When I build things here, I have to do it all myself!

On May 4, 2010, at 11:45 AM, MartinMods wrote:

"We're developing a neck that is a continuous, seamless tube, from tip to tenon."

My new neck is a continuous, seamless tube, from tip to body.
FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Before we go too far down this road of conjecture, I would like to
introduce  Gilbert's study
<http://perso.univ-lemans.fr/%7Ejgilbert/ArticlesPageWeb/2008_Nief&al_JA\
SA_Influence_wall_vib.pdf>   on wall vibrations.   This is the most
conclusive study to date involving thin walled reed instruments and is
directly on point to this discussion which is the  effects of wall
vibrations on the vibrating air column inside the instrument.

I think it is also important to note the distinction between the solid
tube brass wind instruments and the tone hole lattice thin walled
conical woodwinds such as saxophones.

A veritable gold mine of Gilbert's published studies can be found at
this link.  Joel Gilbert <http://perso.univ-lemans.fr/%7Ejgilbert/>   
Enjoy.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Is is well to remember that the latest research on wall vibrations, using tubes 30x thinner than those of thinwall flutes, showed no significant coupling at playing frequencies. When the tube was forced to enter breathing mode by making it elliptical, spectral analysis showed no significant
 differences in the final sound caused by a heavily vibrating tube.

Toby

MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:                                           I think that David Monet's approach could be applied to saxophones with positive results, i.e., the use of thick massive construction in his "Rava" trumpet, eliminating as much sympathetic wall vibration as
 possible.  Wall vibrations will never be in phase with those of the air column, due to the difference in mass.  They can only cause interference.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
".....wall vibrations of a very thin wall have no significant effect upon the
vibrating air column, then going from a medium wall to a thicker wall
would produce a negligible result."

I'm not sure how many of the 100 billion neurons in the human brain are involved with playing the saxophone.  There are 100 million in the auditory cortex alone.  I think an accomplished player makes playing close to a full-body experience.  That's a lot of information being input every few microseconds.  Besides auditory input, the player is able to discern through the "feel" of actual playing a real saxophone, differences which might not make themselves apparent, using a simplified test "axe" and an artificial embouchure/blowing machine.  What a player experiences, not a laboratory study, is, in the end, the player's reality. 

More controlled player tests needed.
FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
The player may very well experience the vibrations of the neck/mouthpiece in contact the top teeth up through the skull to the auditory canal.  That's part of the reason that we sound different on a recording than when we hear ourselves live.  That has nothing to do with the actual sound that is transmitted to the listener.

--- In MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com, MartinMods <lancelotburt@...> wrote:

> ".....wall vibrations of a very thin wall have no significant effect upon the
> vibrating air column, then going from a medium wall to a thicker wall
> would produce a negligible result."
> 
> I'm not sure how many of the 100 billion neurons in the human brain are involved with playing the saxophone.  There are 100 million in the auditory cortex alone.  I think an accomplished player makes playing close to a full-body experience.  That's a lot of information being input every few microseconds.  Besides auditory input, the player is able to discern through the "feel" of actual playing a real saxophone, differences which might not make themselves apparent, using a simplified test "axe" and an artificial embouchure/blowing machine.  What a player experiences, not a laboratory study, is, in the end, the player's reality.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Certainly, but I'm not referring to that.  And I'm not talking about what the listener hears at all.  I'm taking about what the player notices as differences in the "feel" of the response of the instrument, however fine, which all boils down to, the manner, the speed and ease, in which the horn forms harmonic regimes, and their degree of stability, flexibility, and resultant timbre, as the player goes from one fingering to another, and all points in between, at all dynamic levels, and using all articulations and inflections.  This is something that no such study, nor listening jury can tell you.

FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
I know what you are saying, and a strong argument can be made that even minor changes inside the bore of the instrument can affect this "feel" to the performer who is intimately familiar with the instrument to begin with.  To say that adding (or taking away) mass at various locations on the outside of the tube can affect the harmonicity of certain notes to the extent that the performer can tell a difference is a stretch that has no underlying scientific proof whatsoever.

FROM: saxgourmet (STEVE GOODSON)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Nothing matters except the way the performer interacts with the  
horn.....nothing......all the artificial tests in the world mean  
nothing without real world experience with competent players.....play  
testing by multiple players is the only method that will ever give  
meaningful results, unless you are designing instruments to be played  
by machines as opposed to being played by humans.....

on another note, can you offer any evidence whatsoever that the  
addition or deletion of mass to the exterior of the tube does NOT  
affect response? We have tried it with multiple full time players, and  
they strongly agree that there is a significant difference.

FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test

Vague anecdotal accounts by unnamed players who supposedly claim to be able to tell a difference when mass is added to or removed from the neck does not constitute "evidence" by any definition of the word.

Why not make a recording with the weights on the neck, then quickly unsolder them and make another recording of the same player, same set-up and music being played?

Better yet record long tones throughout the range of the saxophone at several different dynamic levels with and without the brass weights installed and compare the spectrographs of the identical notes played at identical dynamic levels by the same player.

This would not be conclusive evidence by any means, but it would carry a lot more credibility than vague, unsupported, anonymous anecdotal remarks repeated by someone who has an obvious bias and financial interest in the matter.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"To say that adding (or taking away) mass at various locations on the
outside of the tube can affect the harmonicity of certain notes to the
extent that the performer can tell a difference is a stretch that has
no underlying scientific proof whatsoever."

Despite what you imply, the degree of resonance harmonicity or
inharmonicity, is not the only factor which can affect the way an
instrument plays.  Turbulence and damping effects can make an
instrument difficult, or even impossible to play. 

Though you say you know what I am talking about (the importance of the player's physical perception of the instrument's response) you continue to suggest making A/B recordings as audio listening tests as a means to demonstrate these differences.  I still fail to see how making audio recordings can convey my impressions of the physical sensation of playing an instrument.  Only a player will know what I'm talking about.

As Benade advised, the player can readily discern microscopic (0.XX mm) changes in the dimensions of an instrument - upper bore, sharp edges, etc, and these are things that more often than not, only the player will be aware of.  Gibiat reported the negative effects of wall vibrations in a saxophone bell.  I personally heard the great American woodwind performer and educator, Russell Howland, recount his own experience with wall vibrations in the bottom bow of a saxophone - how adding mass to the outside of the bow stabilized the tone.  I would attribute these cases and the rest of what I am concerned about, to turbulence and resultant amplitude loss, rather than wall vibration, coupling induced, harmonicity effects.   Since the f1 resonance is already weak in the saxophone's low register, any turbulence induced amplitude loss can render the low register unplayable.  Any amplitude loss in any regime resonance will alter the tone, response, and
 possibly the pitch of that note.

Since 99% of the input energy is lost to thermal and viscous effects, from the start, I  consider any condition, which could possibly contribute to even the slightest further loss, even if not clearly audible to the listener, to be worthy of serious consideration.  That's just me, as a player, talking, but the history of the development of these instruments shows us that, for the most part, their refinement was a process of many small and sometimes, seemingly insignificant changes, based upon empirical considerations, rather than those scientific.
FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
I would attribute these cases and the rest of what I am concerned about,
to turbulence and resultant amplitude loss, rather than wall vibration,

loss in any regime resonance will alter the tone, response, and possibly
the pitch of that note.

I'm not following your logic and reasoning here Lance.  What are you
claiming the cause of turbulence to be?  How does this relate to the
discussion of the effects of adding mass or thickness to the outside of
the tube?

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"What are you claiming the cause of turbulence to be?  How does this
relate to the discussion of the effects of adding mass or thickness to
the outside of the tube?"

I think there is a point where spelling things out becomes ridiculous.  

Surely you are aware that the horn body vibrates during playing.  Surely you know that an uncoupled vibrating surface will cause the air next to it to vibrate as well, and that air movement will be perpendicular to the surface, and out of phase with the wave that generated the vibration.  Any air molecule motion which is not coupled with the laminar flow of
the wave is turbulence.  Two traveling waves (1. the regime wave and 2.
the turbulence)* which exist in the same medium will interfere with
each other.  This is known as the Interference of Sound.

* my paretheses
FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
You really don't need to be rude in your response to my questions.  I have not come across this phenomenon in any of the literature I have studied to date.  I do know that Benade discusses turbulence only in terms of the sharp edges inside the woodwind instrument.  Can you name a resource or reference where I can learn more about this "turbulence" effect that you describe.  Thank you.

> the wave is turbulence.  Two traveling waves (1. the regime wave and 2.
> the turbulence)* which exist in the same medium will interfere with
> each other.  This is known as the Interference of Sound.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Do a search for "Interference of Sound Waves".

I do not believe that wall vibration can be heard by the listener, but it is conceivable that the player may notice the effects of it interfering with the regime wave to some degree.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
As a place to start, I'll quote the 1st and 3rd sentences, 4th paragraph of the Introduction of Gilbert's study then, which you yourself provided.  Read them as many times as it takes to sink in.  

"Although the fluid-structure interactions in wind instruments are weak, the vibrations of the walls, no matter their origin, may then induce sound radiation in the surrounding external or internal fluid."

"The radiation to the internal fluid may induce a perturbation of the oscillations of the air column and thus disturb indirectly the radiated field."

I thought theoretical discussions involved actually thinking about things, as opposed to limiting oneself to reading and reciting the printed words from a page of text, sine intellectus. :-)

FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
It would be so nice to have a discussion with you without the backbiting
snide remarks Lance.  You might want to try that some time.

Repeating your quotes with added emphasis:

"Although the fluid-structure interactions in wind instruments are weak,
the vibrations of the walls, no matter their origin, may then induce
sound radiation in the surrounding external or internal fluid."

"The radiation to the internal fluid may induce a perturbation of the 
oscillations of the air column and thus disturb indirectly the radiated 
field."

The rest of the study makes it very clear that  "in order to obtain a
measurable effect, the tested tube has to be very thin (.2 mm) and
slightly oval shaped which is not realistic for a wind instrument".
[Gilbert's exact words in quotes]

In his conclusions he writes:

"An artificial oval shaped and thin walled system has been constructed
for these effects to be measurable.  For real orchestral instruments, 
although the previously described mechanism is not to be excluded
completely for a particular instrument,  similar effects are thus
unlikely to be measurable and, even if they were, would only occur for
one or a few notes."

My point here is this.  It is perfectly acceptable for one to create a
theory or hypothesis based upon referenced factual scientific
information.  However, said theory or hypothesis should be clearly
stated as such so as not to be construed as actual scientific
fact---which it is not until it has been proven scientifically.

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
I believe my earlier statement was clear enough:

"I do not believe that wall vibration can be heard by the listener, but it is conceivable that the player may notice the effects of it interfering with the regime wave to some degree."

While Gilbert's study is interesting and seems to be primarily concerned with what could affect the tone color of the sound produced, I don't think it provides the slightest insight into what is or is not noticeable to the accomplished player as "feel" when playing a real saxophone in the real world.

I'm inclined to place more trust in my own, immeasurably more sophisticated, sensitive, and refined bio-testing mechanism, when evaluating or comparing the tonal, dynamic, and responsive characteristics of any real saxophone.

--- In MouthpieceWork2_Acoustics@yahoogroups.com, MartinMods

FROM: lancelotburt (MartinMods)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
"This is the classic Coltman experiment that seems to refute Schmidt's conclusion from his not-so-scientific test."

I see absolutely nothing unscientific about JS's "Materials" test.  He did state that it is intended for the trained ear. Test results from more players would be interesting, but I find no reason to discount his results. 

The Coltman publication states that, "The acoustically important dimensions of all three instruments were then identical within 0.01 cm."  That's 0.004".  Flutes and mouthpieces today are machined to within 0.002" or less in critical areas.  I use 0.005" brass sheet to make resonators.  It's not so thin that it doesn't exist.
FROM: jbtsax (John)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test

I was waiting for Toby to field this one.  For starters Jim Schmidt who has an obvious bias to begin with did both the playing and the listening part of the test himself.  It is also highly likely that even though he was blindfolded and used one hand to hold the headjoint to his lip that he could detect a different feel in the "swing" of weighted flute versus the lighter one.

For Schmidt's test to have any scientific validity, it would need to be duplicated with several professional flutists playing the apparatus with a panel of experienced listeners in a series of double blind tests with a large enough number of trials to determine statistically the validity and accuracy of the responses. 

Also adding a large and unnatural weight to the end of a thin walled flute can hardly be compared to making the entire body of the flute out of a thicker or denser metal making the conclusion of the study as flawed as the methodology.

FROM: kymarto (kymarto123@...)
SUBJECT: Re: Functional Mouthpiece Volume Range Test
Hi,

I was in the Philippines working my butt off, with no internet. 

I corresponded directly with Jim about this, and it was I who pushed him to try a blindfold test. I also criticized his results for the same reason that John mentions. Jim said that he could detect a difference four out of five times in his blindfold test with suspended flute. This alone seems to
 indicate that the differences were not large to begin with. After he published his results, 

I wrote to him that to be more rigorous, he had to clamp the flute in a way that he could not swing it, as subtle weight differences are a big tipoff. I told him that I was pretty sure that if he removed weight clues he would no longer be able to tell the instruments apart. Jim said that he would
 redo the test as I suggested and revise his results. I believe that the fact that he did not do so is significant.

Toby
